I. THE AGRICULTURAL SECTOR

had dropped to 11%, replaced by soybeans and soybean products as the country's
largest foreign exchange earner. The Brazilian cattle population had reached
89 million head by 1979, with some 38 million pigs, 310 million chickens and
considerable numbers of horses,, goats and sheep. The 1980 milk production was
estimated around 10 million tons, after a rapid growth period between 1972 and
1976. Some 10.7 million cattle were slaughtered in 1980 and the poultry
industry has been growing extremely rapidly. Wieat has recently accounted for
one-third of the value of Brazil's agricultural imports (3.8 million tons of
wheat imported in 1979). Other principal agricultural import commodities
include natural rubber, live animals for breeding, temperate zone fruits, milk
and certain meats and seeds. Bad crop years result in periodic importation of
basic food crops such as maize, rice and beans (US$1 billion in 1980).
B. Government Agricultural Policies
1.03      Agricultural development has been assigned a high priority in
Brazil's 1980-1985 development plan to (a) help dampen inflation through
expanded food production and stronger transport and marketing systems; and
(b) alleviate the balance-of-payments constraints through increased
production of export crops and energv substitutes. To help achieve these
objectives the Government has reinforced its support of special regional
programs. Nonetheless, due to the high inflation rate and the public sector
deficits, the Government is restraining public expenditure and also trying to
reduce the very high agricultural credit subsidies. Despite a series of
recent medsures, however, Lhe ielative rate of subsidy on regular rural credit
remains quite high. Even more heavily subsidized specific crop incentive
programs remain available, such as those to produce natural rubber. Regionally,
credit distribution is biased in favor of more modern agriculture in the
South. Also, about 80% of crop-specific credit has gone to six crops (soybeans,
wheat, rice, maize, coffee and sugarcane) which account for 60% of gross
agricultural production.
1.04      For the past several years, the minimum price program has set
guaranteed floor prices for 42 commodities to (a) help farmers make planting
decisions; (b) provide for the establishment of Government regulatory stocks;
and (c) ensure stable feed prices to livestock producers. Although minimum
prices are periodically adjusted upward in real terms, they still remain
below market prices prevailing at harvest.
1.05      Provision of fertilizers and other inputs and implements is usually
done by the private sector, except in more remote areas where it is handled,
often poorly, by state enterprises. The use of improved seeds, fertilizers
and insecticides remains concentrated in the southern half of Brazil. Consid-
erable scope exists for the expansion of their use in other regions. National
consumption of fertilizers increased at a rate of 16% per year between 1972
and 1978 and is now slightly above 3 million tons per annum. Some 40% of
nitrogen fertilizers and 70% of phosphoric components are domestically pro-
duced. All potassium fertilizer is imported. Soybean growers are the main
fertilizer users (21%) followed by sugarcane, wheat, coffee, rice and maize,
which account together for another 54% of fertilizer consumption.



-3-
1.06      The principal regional development programs, largely devoted to
providing agricultural services and infrastructure comprise: (a) the Develop-
ment Program for Integrated Areas in the Northeast (POLONORDESTE), aimed at
the rural poor in priority areas of that region; (b) the Development Program
for Integrated Areas of Amazonia (POLAMAZONIA), to help coordinate investment
for agricultural and mineral development in the Amazon basin; and (c) the
Development Program for the Integrated Central Areas (POLOCENTRO) to bring the
vast "cerrado" lands into agricultural production. The Northwest Region
Integrated Development Program (POLONOROESTE), now in the process of establish-
ment, would aim at the further settlement, equitable development and environ-
mental protection of its area of action.
C. Bank Involvement in the Agricultural Sector
1.07      The Bank has so far made 21 loans, totalling US$801.6 million,
for agriculture and rural development in Brazil. These include two, amounting
to US$119.2 million, for agro-industries; two, totalling US$60.5 million,
for livestock development; one for US$18.2 million for grain storage; two
amounting to US$100 million for agricultural research; one for US$100 million
to improve agricultural extension services; and 13, totalling US$403.7 million,
for various settlement, irrigation and rural development projects, 11 of which
are located in the Northeast. In addition, there have been three loans
totalling US$196 million for fertilizer production; a loan of US$19 million
for a nutrition research and development project; a loan of US$32 million for
a Northeast basic education project; and two loans, totalling US$165 million
for secondary and feeder roads. Several additional agricultural and rural
development operations have been recently appraised or are currently at a
relatively advanced state of preparation: a rural development project in the
Northeast state of Maranhao, and two other projects linked to the Northwest
Development Program: a rural development project in Mato Grosso (appraised in
June, 1981) and a project for new settlements in Rondonia and Mato Grosso.
Additional agricultural and rural development projects are also under
consideration for possible future Bank financing. Completion and Project
Performance Audit Reports have been issued for the grain storage and the two
livestock development projects. In each of these three projects, significant
differences developed between the Government and the Bank regarding interest
rate policy, leading to some cancellation and/or early repayments of the
loans. Completion and Project Performance Audit Reports are currently under
preparation for an agroindustry credit project and the Alto Turi land
settlement project (see para 4.04).
II. THE NORTHWEST REGION INTEGRATED DEVELOPMENT
PROGRAM (POLONOROESTE)
A. Objectives and Strategy of POLONOROESTE
2.01      The Northwest Region Integrated Development Program (POLONOROESTE)
originated with the general objectives of paving the road (BR-364) which
already links Cuiaba, the capital of Mato Grosso, to Porto Velho, the capital
of the Tetritory of Rondonia (an area of considerable productive potential,
which has experienced rapid immigration over the last decade), and concomitantly



providing for the investments necessary to achieve the harmonious socio-
economic development of the region influenced by that road and the protection
of the physical environment and Amerindian population of that region. Built
in the 1960s, BR-364 provided the first overland access to most of the region
but is now in very poor condition. Sections of it are closed for weeks
during the long rainy season, and, even when passable, entail very high
transportation costs.
2.02      Paving of the BR-364 highway will increase the economic contribution
of the underutilized physical resources of this region to the national economy,
reducing substantially transport costs and thus improving the region's terms
of trade with the more developed and populated areas lying to the south. The
improving of BR-364 would, however, make its area of influence more accessible
to potential immigrants and increase the pressure on the local physical and
social environment. Hence, policies and corresponding implementation struc-
tures are being set up to help avoid a disorderly development of the region
which could irreversibly place in jeopardy those same resources that paving of
the BR-364 highway would aim at developing. There is little doubt that,
whether BR-364 is paved or not, continuing immigration would, without adequate
administration, result in long term damage to the environment and society
through occupation of poorer soils with ill-adapted agricultural practices,
loss of valuable timber, encroachment onto Amerindian and natural reserves,
agglomeration of land in the hands of relatively few, and a possible agglomera-
tion of migrants in urban areas ill-equipped to receive them.
2.03      The program strategy of seeking reasonable social and environmental
balances in the Northwest region as the BR-364 highway is paved combines
measures of dissuasion to keep immigrants away from areas not yet sufficiently
studied (or those proven to be inadequate) with measures to attract rural
settlers to areas of proven agricultural or forestry potential and to introduce
appropriate cultivation techniques.
2.04      A decree defining the objectives and resources of the program on
the basis of extensive studies conducted in the program area was signed by
the President of the Republic on May 27, 1981. As defined in that decree,
the Northwest Development Program is intended to be implemented between 1981
and 1985. Bank financing of the Program is currently envisaged in three
overlapping phases. Since these phases would each require about five years to
be completed, and an interval of one year is expected to elapse between the
start of the first and third phases, the overall program would require
about six years to be completed. The first and most extensive phase would
include (a) the paving of BR-364; (b) the strengthening of malaria control
and establishment of a network of rural health posts and urban health referral
centers in Rondonia, and health related research; and (c) agricultural development
in Rondonia and environmental protection. The rural development actions initiated
in the latter project would be shortly extended to similar settled areas of Mato
Grosso to provide assistance to some 17,500 small-farmer families in a second phase
of the Northwest program to be soon initiated. A third phase would soon thereafter
aim at gradually developing new planned settlement schemes in unoccupied areas
which would have been identified as having adequate potential, both in Rondonia
1/   The program area is described in paras 3.01 through 3.05 of this report.

in parallel to all three program phases. Selective feeder roads to existing
and planned settlements in Rondonia and Mato Grosso would also be financed in
parallel to the three program phases, through the Highway Loan, or existing
or future feeder road credit lines of the National Economic Development Bank
(BNDE), partly financed by the Bank.

2.07      Funding would be channelled through the various implementation
agencies after approval by the Federal Planning Secretariat (SEPLAN) and
under supervision of the Superintendency for Economic Development in the
Center-West Region (SUDECO), which would coordinate and monitor all program
activities. Funds allocated to agencies of the State of Mato Grosso and the
Territory of Rondonia would be channelled through their respective Planning
Secretariats.
D. Program Benefits
2.08      The preliminary economic assessment of the Northwest development
program is based on a comparison of the "with" and "without program" situa-
tions. The "without" situation assumes no investments in the main or feeder
roads, no institutional strengthening and no additional technical support of
agriculture. Agriculture would expand uncontrolled within its present tech-
nology and yields would gradually decline while access costs would increase.
1/   Includes US$38.2 million for the end segments of the road built by the Army.



- 7 -
The "with program"' scenario incorporates all actions to be undertaken under
the program, generating sustained expanded agricultural production and savings
in vehicle operating costs. The internal rate of return of the program over a
20-year period is above 50%. There are also major expected benefits of the
program of a largely non-quantifiable nature. They include the better protec-
tion of large ecologically sensitive areas (see Annex 7) and of the Amerindian
population, considerable strengthening of the institutional capacity in the
region and improved knowledge of the Amazon Basin environment. There are also
difficult to quantify costs attached to the development of the Northwest
region, such as those associated with invasions of unsuitable soils, potential
endangerment of native flora and fauna species and pressure on previously
isolated Amerindian populations. Since, however, the occupation and develop-
ment process of the region has already substantially begun, such costs would
be far higher if development activities were not guided and coordinated
through the proposed program. Environmental monitoring built into the program
(see para 4.24) should considerably enhance the capacity to detect and respond
to adverse ecological trends. Details on the economic analysis of the Northwest
program are provided as an annex to the Northwest I - Highways Project Staff
Appraisal Report.
III. THE PROJECT AREA
A. The Northwest Program Area
General
2
3.01      The Northwest Region of Brazil covers approximately 410,000 km
about three quarters the size of France, though slightly less than 5% of the
Brazilian territory. It includes the entire federal Territory of Rondonia and
fourteen western municipalities of the state of Mato Grosso, all officially
considered within the area of influence of the Cuiaba-Porto Velho highway,
BR-364 (Map No. 14865R1). The regional population was estimated in the neighbor-
hood of 1,000,000 in 1980 and has been growing at 10.8% per year during the
last decade (7.8% per year in Mato Grosso and 15.8% per year in Rondonia). The
average population density of the region is still less than three inhabitants
per km2 (1.6 if the city of Cuiaba/Varzea Grande is excluded), mostly con-
centrated in cities and agricultural settlements built along BR-364. The
dominant feature of the region is the east-west oriented ridge of the Serra
dos Parecis (about 600 m), continued in Rondonia by the Serra dos Pacaas Novos,
separating the Amazon basin, north, from the River Plate basin, south. North
and east of the Serra dos Parecis is an ancient sandstone plateau. The
natural landscape of Rondonia is of virtually uninterrupted tropical moist
forest, giving way to grass land and swamps on poorer soils to the south-west.
North of the Pacaas Novos ridge, rainfall is near or above 2,000 mm. A
central settlement area of some three million hectares, established by the
National Institute of Colonization and Agrarian Reform (INCRA) during the
last ten years has large patches of very fertile soils. Older settlements,
resulting mainly from private colonization, are to be found within the nine
municipalities of Caceres, Mirassol d'Oeste, Jauru, Salto do Ceu, Quatro
Marcos, Rio Branco, Araputanga, Barra do Bugres and Tangara da Serra in Mato
Grosso. They lie over a landscape starting in tropical forest in the west and
gradually shifting to "cerrado" or savannah (1,600 mm rainfall) and the highly
specialized environment of the seasonally flooded Pantanal (1,200 mm rainfall)
to the south-east. A few very large ranches established with Government
incentives (which have since been abolished) occupy a large segment of the



- 8 -
Guapore va'lley along the Mato Grosso border with Bolivia, with several rela-
tively small Nambikwara Indian reserves scattered among them. Extensive
Amerindian and natural reserves are found north of the central stretch of the
highway, and the very large municipality of Aripuana on the Northwestern
corner of NHat  Grosso remains virtually uninhabited.
3.02     Socio-economic development of Rorndonia is almost exclusively
concentrated along the BR-364 axis, mainly within the area of the Settlement
Consolidation subproject. The Territory of Rondonia is expected to achieve
statehood by early 1982. Until then its administration will remain under
Federal authority. No major modifications are expected to result in the
structures or functions of existing institutions. However, their staff is
expected to be considerably strengthened, and while the state gradually
develops fts own financial base, federal funding would remain available
during a transition period of a few years.
L.and Capabili tv and Use
3.03      The N\Torthwest region is estimated to have some 29,000 km  (about
7% of the total) oL good eutrophic podsols, mainly in Rondonia.  A further
half of the remaining soils are estimated to be capable of sustaining agricul-
ture with liming and fertilizers. The better soils are found mainly in
central and southern Rondonia, the western part of Aripuana and the southern
Guapore Valley and the Mirassol d'Oeste region in Mato Grosso. As development
in these areas is very new, however, there will clearly be a need for systematic
research and evaluation of the most effective means to treat production
limitations which could emerge and which could affect returns on production in
certain of those areas. In many areas, tree crops are likely to be preferred,
on environmental grounds, over annuals or cattle grazing.  In 1978 about 1%
of the region was planted in either annual or tree crops, 14% was in natural
or seeded pasture (mostly in the savannah and Pantanal); less than 5% was
under forestry operations; and about 80% remained in its natural state.
Less than 5% of the forest area had been cleared up to 1980, and while the
pace of deforestation has increased in recent years, there remain millions of
hectares of virgin forest which could be developed into productive national
forests, national parks or biological reserves.

Amerindians and Amerindian Reserves
3.04      As many as 8,000 Amerindians are estimated to be living in the
Northwest region, within 22 knowhn tribal groups of different size, language,
culture and degree of acculturation to Brazilian society. Some have not yet
been contacted. About 4,600 of them live in 5.3 million ha (13% of the
region) which have been designated official Amerindian areas. Only a third
of that area has been actually demarcated, and the presence of squatters and
other intruders within many Amerindian reserves has been a recurrent problem,
especially in the Sete de Setembro reserve in the north-east of Rondonia,
where invading settlers have clashed with the indigenous population over the
last decade and are now being evicted through Government action. In the
Guapore Valley, in Mato Grosso, the establishment of large ranches was accom-
panied by displacements of Nambikwara tribes and numerous redefinitions of
their legal reserves (as tribes displaced to environments totally different
from their own gradually made their way back to their forests of origin).
While the overall situation of these groups is still precarious, relative
improvements during the last few years have resulted in a reversal of the
previous decline in their population. A group of Amerindian tribes, globally
identified as the uru-eu-wau-wau, are known to dwell in the central areas of
Rondonia and are now in the process of being contacted. The National Indian
Foundation (FUNAI), has established 26 Amerindian posts in the Northwest
region, through which direct contacts with the indigenous population take
place. However, few of the posts are yet adequately staffed or equipped, and
12 of them had no staff at all in 1980. Three mobile health teams, one also
attending areas outside the region, carry out routine and emergency missions.
(Measures to strengthen the FUNAI program in the region and described in
Annex 8).
Natural Reserves and Ecological Stations
3.05      Official natural reserves in the region total some 2 million ha,
or 5% of the region. They are placed under the management of the Brazilian
Institute for Forestry Development (IBDF). The main ones are (a) the
Pacaas Novos Park (765,000 ha in Rondonia); (b) the Jaru Biological Reserve
(268,000 ha in Rondonia); and (c) the Cara Cara Biological Reserve (80,000 ha
in the Mato Grosso Pantanal). A Biological Reserve of 500,000 ha in the
Guapore valley awaits a decree establishing it. Surrounded by cliffs, the
Pacaas Novos Park is rather isolated from the plain below. It is extremely
significant for conservation because of the fertile soils it contains, cri-
tical to supporting the flora and fauna found mainly in those areas of Rondonia
where settlement and forest clearing occur. A 200,000 ha ecological station
established by the Special Secretariat for the Environment linked to the
Ministry of Interior (SEMA) in Mato Grosso just outside the Northwest region
would be used as a model for similar stations proposed within the program
(see para 4.23). Aside from the reserves, one of the main conservation measures
is the rule, often difficult to enforce, prohibiting private land owners
from clearing more than 50% of their holding. To improve the prospects for
strict enforcement of the principle that land should be held in reserve, INCRA
is considering replacing individual reserves in future settlement areas with
an equivalent agglomerated reserve. The legal status of such reserves has not
yet been defined.



- 10 
B, Rondonia Settlement Consolidation Subproject Area
General
3.06      The five INCIA. settlement schemes of Burareiro, Marechal Dutra,
Adolpho Rohl, Ourc Preto and Gy-Parana, where most of the subproject infra-
structure and services would be concentrated, straddle BR-364 over some 350 km
(Map No. 15483). They are located within the six municipalities of Ariquemes,
Ji-Parana, Cacoal, Presidente Medici, Ouro Preto do Oeste and Jaru. 1/
Today, some 37,000 families (more than half of the farming population of
Rondonia) live within the 3 million ha (about 12% of the Territory area) of
these schemes, including areas of spontaneous settlement where land tenure is
now being regularized in the periphery of the original 2.2 million ha settle-
ments. The typical settler is provided with a 100 ha plot, including the 50%
forest -eserve.  Of the 3 million ha, only 117,000 ha are in annual crops,
80,000 ha ir tree crops and 63,000 ha in natural pasture.  The remaining area
is mostly undisturbed dense or open tropical forest. Most settlers come from
the southern states of Brazil.  They have farming experience, especially with
coffee, but bring little else with them. The average family of 4.8 people
is estimated to have the equivalent of 2.2 adults of available labor. There
is strong demand for hired labor, especially during harvesting season.
Climate and Soils
3.07      The subproject area lies between 100 and 400 m above sea-level.
It has a humid5 tropical climate, monthly mean temperatures of 210 to 26  GC,
rainfall between 1,800 and 2,200 mm and three to four months with less than
50 mm rainfall coinciding with the cooler nights of the year, which reduce
the risk of some plant diseases, The terrain is gently undulating, often
dissected by streams or rivers.  Up to 1.8 million ha of eutrophic red-yellow
podsols of usually considerable depth found in the general area of the sub-
project are unusualiy fertile for the Amazon basin. The better soils of the
area are mostly found in the southern settlements.  Soils oF the Ariquemes
region requ re more careful management. Altogether, some two thirds of the
area are wall- suited to long cycle crops, half of that area being also
suitable for annual crops, with proper cultivation techniques and adequate
use of fert-llizers  Masp 15482)   The standing timber volume varies between
100 and '20     -per hectare (70 to 93 m3/ha classified as marketable, of which
some 1.5 to 25 M3/haa of export quality, some already extracted).
Phvsical and Social  Infrastructure
3.08      The road  wi-rastructure of the area follows an orthogonal pattern with
feeder roads stemm.ing out of BR-364 every 15 to 30 km and cut perpendicularly
by access roads everv four kilometers.  The access road network, intended
to have an extension of some 6,400 km (0.22 km/km2), is only half constructed,
in various stndards and with inadequate maintenance.  Since 1979, the Rondonia
SecretartaL o  tgri- ulture (SEAC) and, more recently, its successor, the Rondonia
Agricultiural DeveLopment Company (CODARON), have opened and maintained some

- 11 -
3,500 km of farm access roads using community labor complemented with contracted
equipment. Local contractors are currently employing a total fleet of some 50
D6 and D8 bulldozers in the subproject area under the planning and supervision
of CODARON. The 1981 objective of CODARON is to open 1,500 km of new roads
and rehabilitate another 1,500 km of previously built roads. By early July
1981, approximately half of that objective had been reached. Under the heavy
local rainfall, however, these roads (as currently constructed) would need
complete reconstruction every two years. There are some 580 rural primary
schools in the three municipalities of the project area, attending about
27,000 students, or one-third of the relevant age population, under totally
inadequate conditions. There are also 12 main health posts and 21 mini-posts
with a total of 13 doctors and ten nurses. The incidence of malaria in the
area is very high.
Land Tenure
3.09      Forty four percent of the 33,500 owner farmers of the area
had received definitive titles by the end of 1980, and an accelerated
titling program is expected to provide titles to all remaining plot owners
by the end of 1981. Under the present conditions, a family can cultivate
up to a maximum of 10 ha, of which not more than half in perennial crops,
relying on outside labor only during peak season. Beyond that, better-off
farmers seek permanent outside labor, and the farmer population is gradually
becoming stratified between (a) owners who have successfully planted signifi-
cant areas of perennial crops giving them a high income potential; (b) less
successful owners who have yet to establish a perennial crop; and (c) non-
owners, whose opportunities remain limited to provision of labor or to share-
cropping. There are, today, some 3,500 non-owner families. The number of
sharecropper families is, however, higher as some are also squatters in the
periphery of the settlements. By the end of the project investment period,
the area population is expected to increase to some 75,000 families, at least
half of wlhich would be non-owners.
Agricultural Production
3.10      A new settler will normally sell the most valuable timber on his
lot (about 2% of the total timber) and fell the remaining forest of lesser
logging value by axe or chainsaw, burning it where it falls. The main annual
crops -- rice, maize and beans -- are grown by low-cost, low risk (and low
yield) traditional methods relying on family labor. They are often inter-
planted among immature coffee or cocoa. Pasture is often planted after
annual crops, but, due to the cost, cattle on these farms are still quite
rare. The main cash crops are coffee -- half in arabica planted without
government credit and half in robusta financed by the Brazilian Coffee
Institute (IBC) since 1976 -- and cocoa, with rubber recently on the increase.
Tree crops establish rapidly, and coffee and cocoa bear early and heavily.
Coffee leaf rust, witches broom disease of cocoa and the South American Leaf
Blight fungus on rubber are present, but tested control mechanisms exist.
They have been successfully used in other regions of Brazil, including the
Amazon basin, where they have proven financially acceptable to the farmers.
Coffee and cocoa production methods are satisfactory, similar to those
elsewhere in Brazil. Areas under annual and tree crops both rose by some
30% between 1979 and 1980. No planted rubber has yet reached maturity.
Once land title is provided, the main constraints on agricultural production
will be high transport costs and difficult access to services. Post-harvest
losses of rice which matures during the rainy season, when it cannot be
moved from the farm, are estimated at 25 to 40%. Farmgate prices are often
far below official prices for the same reason. Annual income of settler



- 12 -
families is quite variable, averaging now an estimated US$1,000 for owners and
US$700 for non-owners. Furthermore, the relative purchasing power of these
incomes is somewhat less than in the rest of Brazil given the high cost of
many products in the isolated areas of Rondonia.

Institutional Background
3.11      The management of the five settlement schemes was originally
the sole responsibility of INCRA. In the late 1970s, Territorial and other
Federal institutions became directly involved in the schemes, absorbing some
of the INCRA staff. Agricultural extension services are currently provided
by the Executive Commission for the Cocoa Development Plan (CEPLAC) for
cocoa, by SEAC for coffee planting and rubber, and the relatively weak
Rondonia affiliate of the national extension company (ASTER-RO) for coffee
maintenance and all other crops. The Brazilian Agricultural Research
Enterprise (EMBRAPA) has a station at Porto Velho and a trials facility at
Ouro Preto. As extension and research institutions have been in operation
in the area for relatively short periods of time, their staff are still of
uneven experience and training. The main thrust of various extension
programs has been on the administration of credit, and the number of farmers
effectively reached per agent has been relatively low. Recently, ASTER-RO
has begun to emphasize extension to the lower income farmers and has initia-
ted a network of 50 demonstration sites as focal points for group extension.
Emphasis is still on credit, however, rather than planning for optimal overall
use of resources available to the farmer. Public storage units, owned and
operated by the Brazilian Storage Company (CIBRAZEM) and acting as delivery
points for the Government minimum price purchase scheme, have been constantly
underutilized, mainly due to access problems. Five new stores (including
driers and cleaners) are being built by CIBRAZEM within the subproject area
qdO8xtmrore around it, bringing total storage volume in the region to about



- 13 -
Input Supply, Credit and Marketing
3.12       Coffee and cocoa seeds cleaned and classified at Central Processing
Units are provided to growers under IBC and CEPLAC crop credit programs. SEAC
operates about 150 ha of rubber nurseries, and ASTER-RO trains some private
sector seed producers. Fertilizers are seldom used on annual crops and the
supply system is weak. Pesticides are sold by private merchants in area towns.
The Bank of Brazil (BB) is the main Government credit agency in the region,
with Bank of Amazonia (BASA) participating in rubber financing. These banks
have few outlets in the area, creating access problems for farmers, who,
furthermore, have traditionally had to apply separately for credit for each
crop they grow. Despite recent increases in nominal interest rates, these
rates remain negative in real terms. There are producer cooperatives in the
three main towns of the area, mainly concerned with marketing cocoa, some rice
and, in the future, coffee. Much of the area production is sold, however, to
intermediaries buying at the farm gate at prices well below the going market
rate in Brazil.
IV. THE PROJECT
A. General Description
Project Origins, Components and Objectives
4.01      The Northwest I Agricultural Development and Environmental Pro-
tection Project is one of the three projects proposed for Bank financing under
the first phase of the Northwest Region Development Program. It consists of
the following subprojects: (a) a Settlement Consolidation subproject to
increase the productivity and develop the migrant absorption and agricultural
production capacity of already settled areas of the Federal Territory of
Rondonia where such potential clearly exists and could be further developed
at minimal environmental costs; (b) an Environmental Protection subproject
to (i) establish natural reserves and ecological stations in areas of the
Northwest of more fragile ecology or with specially diverse fauna and flora;
(ii) strengthen deforestation control capability; and (iii) study the promo-
tion of sustainable forestry operations in suitable areas; (c) an Ecological
Research subproject to (i) study and monitor the hydrometeorology and biogeo-
chemical balances in soils of the program region; (ii) carry out an inventory
of plant and animal species native to Rondonia; and (iii) small scale research
projects, possibly including research on minimum ecosystem sizes; (d) assistance
to INCRA to ensure timely land tenure regularization in settled rural areas of
western Mato Grosso; (e) assistance to INCRA to identify new areas with suitable
soil potential for future settlement; and (f) a component to strengthen overall
program coordination and monitoring capability. In addition, parallel to this
project, but as part of the POLONOROESTE program, the Federal Government would
carry out with its own funds a program to ensure the protection of the Amerindian
population (detailed in Annex 8). Studies on means to improve salvage logging
in settlement areas would be financed in parallel with the assistance of FAO
(detailed in Annex 6). The Government would also study the feasibility of
increasing the density of settlement and agricultural development in the
occupied areas of highest agricultural potential in Rondonia (para 4.18).



- 14 -
Project Rationale and Design
4.02      The Rondonia Settlement Consolidation Subproject.  The agricultural
aptitude and present land tenure situation in the area is conducive (provided
that adequate support measures are taken) to the establishment of a large
number of sustainable middle-income farms. By improving the productive
infrastructure of already settled areas, where production today lags far
behind the sustainable capacity of the land, considerable demand can be
generated for further labor, thus absorbing, at a relatively low cost, part
of the demographic pressure which would otherwise tend to spread out onto less
suitable areas of the region. Such an approach would represent a noteworthy
departure from the subsidized latifundia found in certain areas elsewhere in
the Amazon basin and would have a much higher chance of success than previous
attempts at settling the Amazon region which were located on poorer soils and
were often inadequately planned. Most farmers living in the Rondonia settle-
ments are today among the poorer farmers of Brazil, formerly sharecroppers or
small farmers in other parts of the country, who have only recently settled in
the area. Many of them have not yet benefitted from agricultural services.
Reflecting both a more recent arrival to the area and more difficult access to
basic services, farmers further away from the main road are today, in general,
less successful, with a smaller area cleared and planted and subject to
greater hardship than farmers settled nearer to BR-364. However, their
pioneering spirit and dynamism make it probable that, within not too long a
time, many of those who would obtain legal title to land and would be located
within reasonable reach of credit, extension and marketing services, would be
capable of achieving a much higher level of income. Those few farmers who are
already located relatively near existing agricultural service centers could be
expected to improve their income considerably even in the absence of the
subproject, albeit at higher environmental and social costs. The intention
of the subproject is therefore to assist the Government of Rondonia in its
high priority effort to expand the reach of fundamental agricultural services
to some 18,200 of the less favored, recent settlers found in outlying areas
and non-owner farmers, to help these farmers make better long term use of
their land and labor resources, increase their production and move towards
commercial agriculture based on environmentally more acceptable perennial
cropping and continuous rather than shifting cultivation of annual crops.
The subproject would provide for (a) restructured and reinforced agricultural
extension, applied research and better inputs; (b) reductions in crop losses
through additional and improved transport, storage and basic processing
infrastructure; (c) farmer organization and training, improved land transfer
mechanisms and selective siting of major rural services and infrastructure
so as to direct and limit agricultural development to the better soils of
the area; (d) improved outreach and effectiveness of social services; and
(e) development of the planning and implementation capability of local
institutions. Through these actions, the project aims to create agricul-
tural production and land tenure conditions conducive to an increase of
agricultural population density in the areas with good soils. The subproject
also has considerable potential for institution-building, at a time when the
Territory of Rondonia is about to achieve statehood. The structures proposed
to be developed in the subproject area were designed to provide at least
the minimum infrastructure capable of sustaining private initiative on which
the ultimate development of the region will rely.



- 15 -
4.03      The subproject assumes the improvement of BR-364 and construction
of feeder roads through parallel but distinct actions. In the settlement
consolidation subproject, the Territory would undertake an integrated program
of investment to upgrade farm access roads and expand Government production
support services and social infrastructure which would be grouped mainly at
39 Rural Service Centers (NUAR). NUARs would be sited at strategic points in
the interior of the colonization schemes and linked to BR-364 by existing
or proposed feeder roads. NUARs would act as new foci for community development
and commercial activity.   Each would serve by 1985 some 1,900 rural families
spread over 600 km2, providing them with a social center where religious,
education and leisure needs would also be met.
4.04      Drawing from experience from other rural development projects in
Brazil, the Settlement Consolidation subproject is focused on the least
developed farmers of the area, giving priority assistance to first-time credit
requests. The project design also calls for the personal involvement of area
farmers in various community investments. More specific experience obtained
from the recently completed Alto Turi settlement project shows the importance
of a number of measures which have received special attention in the design of
this project, such as (a) development of a full range of environmental pro-
tection measures at the outset; and (b) active participation of executing
agencies in project preparation, to achieve a broader consensus on project
design and objectives, adequate funding arrangements and clear compatibility
of project objectives with general Government development priorities.  Further-
more, a broader range of production systems are proposed for Rondonia, and these
systems are not new to the region. Finally, project activities built around
community participation would count on traditions already established in the
territory.
4.05      The Environmental Protection and Ecological Research Subprojects.
Humid tropical ecosystems are in general far more complex and less resilient
than other terrestrial ecosystems. High year-round temperatures and heavy
rainfall can result in nutrient losses due to rapid soil decomposition and
leaching. Nutrients are held within the dense vegetation, which quickly
captures them from the soil and water before they are leached away. Tropical
forest has a greater number of different plant and animal species than other
forests, and in the Amazon basin, a large number of these species have their
entire population confined to very small areas 1/ and therefore could risk
total extinction as a result of extensive forest clearing. Knowledge of eco-
systems specific to the Northwest region, particularly in Rondonia, is still
very sparse because of difficult access, especially until the unpaved BR-364
was built in the 1960s, and because knowledge of the ecosystems cannot always
be extrapolated from other Amazon regions. Furthermore, as half of the rain
in the Amazon basin is believed to originate from within the basin itself,
fears have been expressed that extensive disappearance of the tree cover
might affect the climate of the region through changes in evapo-transpiration.
Further fears have been expressed that the release of carbon dioxide by
extensive burning or decomposition of the forest could affect world climate.
Preliminary base studies and a program for systematic environmental monitoring
would allow, however, the timely detection of adverse ecological modifications.
1/   This is due to the forest having receded into isolated patches during
drier periods of the geological past, each patch having developed
distinctive ecologies. Several of these refuges were in Rondonia.



- 16 -
Finally, the Northwest region's soils of moderate quality have considerable
potential for sustained and economically justified forestry activities. The
Environmental Protection subproject aims therefore at (a) strengthening the
action, in the region, of IBDF, which is responsible for the protection of
forests, especially in areas of poor soils, deforestation control and forestry
development; (b) financing the basic infrastructure and surveillance of three
Natural Parks and the further demarcation of one of them; (c) financing the
preparation of plans for national forests; and (d) setting up four ecological
stations under management of SEMA, where baseline ecological studies could be
carried out. In parallel, the ecological research program outlined in para 4.24
would seek to provide timely information to improve land use and conservation
decisions. It would also provide a network of environmental monitoring stations
and enhance basic knowledge of the Amazon environment and of technological
possibilities based on local resources. Such research would be complementary
to similar activities already being implemented elsewhere in the Amazon basin.
4.06      Program Coordination and Other Subprojects.  The Program Coordi-
nation subproject would aim at strengthening the capacity of SUDECO to
administer and monitor the POLONOROESTE program and contract and supervise
ad-hoc supporting studies. The project would also finance land tenure regu-
larization in Mato Grosso in the areas where the second phase and a portion of
the third phase of the POLONOROESTE program would be implemented (Mato Grosso
Rural Development and INCRA settlements). Such regularization is important
for the successful implementation of the proposed second phase project.
Similarly, the project would help finance soil surveys being conducted on behalf
of INCRA as part of their detailed planning of the third phase of the program
(new settlements).
B. Detailed Features
Rondonia Settlement Consolidation Subproject
4.07      Farm Access Roads.  The subproject would provide for the construc-
tion or rehabilitation by CODARON of some 3,900 km of all-weather access roads
linking individual farmsito NUARs or to feeder roads linking these NUARs to
the main highway. All-weather roads are required because part of the harvest
must be marketed during the rainy season, and agricultural extension must
reach farmers mainly during that season. Community labor (mutirao) would be
used for land clearing (up to a width of 30 m according to the orientation of
the road, to provide proper insolation) and construction of simple bridges and
culverts using logs.  Tht. road bed would be raised, gravelled and compacted
using small regional contractors' machinery, thus greatly increasing durability
and lowering maintenance costs. The roadway would be four meters wide, and the
simple design standards atuned to the relatively low traffic volume expected
(15 to 20 five-ton trucks per day at peak harvest months plus a few commercial,
official and private vehicles). CODARON is already actively involved in access
road construction and maintenance in the subproject area (see para 3.08).
The Rondonia Roads Department (DER-RO) of the Secretariat of Public Works and
Services (SOSP) would assist CODARON in the design, tendering and construction
supervision of access roads. DER-RO is planned to be substantially strengthened
under a US$40 million component of the parallel Northwest I - Highways project.
Ten technical support units (included in CODARON staff referred to in para
6.07) would be established to provide field level planning and supervision
of the road work and NUAR infrastructure, each unit having an engineer, two



- 17 -
foremen and apppropriate maintenance equipment. A condition of sub-project
disbursement would be that agreements had been signed between CODARON, DER-RO,
SOSP and the subproject area municipalities (a) defining their collaboration
on access road construction and maintenance; and (b) establishing a plan and
schedule for construction of the higher quality feeder roads (not included in
this project) that is compatible with this project's NUAR and access roads
construction schedule. The implementation of the first 500 km of feeder roads
in Rondonia would be financed as part of the Northwest I - Highways project
within the Territory's 1,530 km feeder road program. Assurances were received
from the Territorial Government of Rondonia that all access roads in the sub-
project area would be adequately maintained. Timely funding would be provided
under the Highway Project for the preparation of remainder of the proposed
parallel feeder roads program to be financed under a proposed Third Feeder Roads
Project. Maintenance of access roads would be provided by each municipality,
assisted by DER-RO. The municipalities already possess road maintenance equipment.
Further needs would be met through DER-RO. Operation and maintenance of a limited
amount of project infrastructure which spreads out of the boundaries of the six
municipalities where most settlers are located would be entrusted to these six
municipalities.
4.08      NUAR Infrastructure.  Each NUAR would initially occupy an area of
about 40 ha at the intersection of two roads. NUAR zoning would include
administration and service areas, some 200 lots for private houses, and recrea-
tional and commercial areas. The NUARs would, hence, provide the core for the
enhanced development of stable communities in the now more remote areas.
The subproject would finance the basic infrastructure on 39 NUAR sites, which
would be destumped and provided each with a deep well, a public water supply
system, a staff dormitory-cum-guest house, a multi-grade school (see para
4.13) and a health post, the later to be financed through the parallel health
project of the Northwest program. For most NUARs, the subproject would
include installation of small-scale sawmills, a generator of 160 KVA operated
by a wood-fired steam engine, a public electricity distribution system and a
crop drying and storage unit (see para 4.11). Except for the storage unit,
CODARON would be responsible for the construction of basic NUAR infrastructure.
CODARON would use community labor and small-scale contracts with regional
enterprises for NUAR construction work. Private trade and services, especially
for the provision of various farm inputs and tools, are expected to rapidly
develop in these centers. Experience in locations already designated for NUAR
construction shows that the more enterprising merchants establish their shops
in the NUARs even before the basic infrastructure is provided.
4.09      Agricultural and Forestry Extension.  The proposed component
would directly assist some 18,200 project beneficiary families (owners or
sharecroppers), to improve their agricultural production and forest utiliza-
tion techniques with particular attention to soil and forest conservation and
to the promotion of ecologically well-suited cultivation of perennial crops.
An intensive training program would be initiated for farmer beneficiaries as
well as project officials. ASTER-RO would be the executing agency, and
the project would finance (a) vehicles and equipment for an expanded agricul-
tural extension system; (b) training courses for 50 new or existing exten-
sionists during each of the first three project years; (c) training courses



- 18 -
for about 1,200 farmer leaders and 1,600 farm youth during the five project
years; (d) appropriate farmer training in pruning, grafting, fumigation and
use of other agricultural equipment; 1/ (e) salaries of the incremental staff;
(f) incremental operating costs of NUAR, municipal and central extension offices,
during the five years of project implementation; and (g) establishment and
operation of about 200 demonstration plots on farmer land to be selected on
the basis of adaptive research results for different soil types, with a view
to demonstrating various possibilities of soil management (improved seeds,
fertilizer use, crop rotation, use of legumes, etc.). On perennial crops
ASTER-RO and CEPLAC would transfer to the farmers technical packages which
have already been developed for each crop by their respective specialized
agencies. Extension staff would be increased from about 45 to 155 officers at
NUAR level plus seven subject matter specialists at regional offices and two
central administrators. The administrative support staff would be increased
by about 50 people. Advice in arrangement of credit would be brought to small
farmers through ASTER-RO. Cocoa extension operations would remain under
CEPLAC, but coordinated with ASTER-RO activities. All other extension activities
would gradually be aggregated under ASTER-RO. The Territorial Government
would consolidate in ASTER-RO all agricultural extension activities, except
those related to cocoa planting and maintenance, and that necessary measures
would be taken to complete recruitment (or transfer) and training of project-
financed staff required by ASTER-RO no later than June 30, 1984. Farmer
training programs and demonstration plots would be managed by ASTER-RO.
Extension services would be provided through (a) individual contact with
direct project beneficiaries, during two consecutive years, to assist them in
farm planning, cultivation techniques and credit requests; and (b) group
extension and general orientation reaching other area farmers as well.
Initial contact points for group extension would be the commissions initially
set up for community road building (see para 4.12), each of which, by the end
of the disbursement period, is expected to include about 60 plot owners (not
necessarily direct project beneficiaries) and their sharecroppers. Each
extension agent would, in the first year of the project, assist some 25
farmers directly and another 50 through group extension. At full development,
these figures would reach about 75 and 200 respectively. The extension agent
would spend roughly half his time with the commissions and the balance on
individual visits, credit work, special field campaigns and the like. The
agent would also receive regular training from senior subject matter specialists
and EMBRAPA researchers, who would, between them, be responsible for formulating
appropriate extension messages for farmers. Besides a general course in
extension techniques, this training would focus on each crop of interest to
the subproject and on various issues related to subproject implementation.
4.10      Agricultural Research.  Complementing the agricultural research
being carried out under the Bank financed First (Loan 1249-BR) and recently
approved Second (Loan 2016-BR) Agricultural Research Projects, which already
include research on perennial crops such as rubber, agricultural research

will materialize only after the five-year project implementation period.



- 19 -
under the project would be oriented to the short-term needs of the settlers
within the project area. It would mainly concentrate on the adaptation of the
main annual crops (corn, rice and beans) to conditions in the newly opened
region, and also on farming system research to be initiated with a view to
collect, as early as possible, more information about the optimal crop mixture
for small and medium farms. At least half of the experiments would be conducted
at farm level. Provisions would be made for the establishment of (a) three
demonstration farms, including perennial crops and livestock; (b) three experi-
mental substations, each of 10 ha, to test new cultivars; (c) three simple
weather observation stations located at EMBRAPA research stations; and (d) soil
tests and mapping services to support an improved land use policy and to provide
the extension service with the required information to train officers in proper
land use practices (including crop rotation) and erosion and environmental
control. To achieve these results, the project would finance vehicles,
equipment, salaries of incremental staff (3 senior and 6 junior research
staff, 12 technicians, 3 administrators and 27 workers), incremental operating
expenses during project implementation, about 20 months of consultancy services
and the yearly publication of results. EMBRAPA's research unit for the
Territory of Rondonia (UEPAT) would be the executing agency. Forestry research
aimed at determining the physical and technical characteristics of the wood
available to the settlers for local use or industrial processing would be
handled through the proposed salvage logging studies (see para 4.17). UEPAT
would publicize each year, in a manner satisfactory to the Bank, the results
of research activities performed under the subproject.
4.11      Crop Drying and Storage.  Crop drying and storage units (which could
also be used for storage of inputs) would be constructed, equipped and staffed
by CIBRAZEM. Standard CIBRAZEM stores have a capacity of about 2,500 tons and
are equipped with grain driers and cleaners. Nineteen additional stores of
such capacity would be required in the project area. They would be sited at
strategically located NUARs, selected among the 34 which do not yet have a
store. Consideration will be given by CIBRAZEM to the design and construction
of smaller 1,500 tons stores, which may better match the need of individual
NUARs, and enable the project to provide each NUAR with its own store within
the present budget of the component. The project would finance construction
and drying, cleaning and handling equipment of at least nineteen warehouses.
CIBRAZEM would hire the personnel and meet all operating costs from storage
fees. A condition of disbursement against the storage component would be that
an agreement be signed between CODARON and CIBRAZEM defining (a) a storage unit
construction plan and schedule consistant with NUAR and access road construction
schedules; and (b) modes of coordination of construction operations between
the store and NUAR managers. Warehouses constructed under the project would
be adequately staffed and maintained by CIBRAZEM.
4.12      Farmer Organization.  The project would further develop the existing
tradition of community labor and the organization of subproject area farmers
through the Secretariat of Social Action (SAS) in Rondonia, working in colla-
boration with CODARON, ASTER-RO and other agencies. Initially, some ten farmer
groups would be organized in the area of influence of each NUAR, coordinated



- 20 -
by local social agents of SAS. These groups of farmers living along a given
section of access road would be trained to participate in various community
activities, such as access road construction and maintenance, 1/ agricultural
extension, nutrition and health education, and school and health post mainte-
nance. It is not intended at this stage to organize the farmers into formal
cooperatives but rather to help in the establishment of "mutirao" (community
action) groups, from which, later, farmer associations and cooperatives could
develop. The project would finance the Secretariat's incremental staffing and
operating costs for these activities.
4.13      Education.  CODARON would construct and equip one school of four
classrooms in each NUAR and 390 single classroom schools along surrounding
access roads. The central schools would initially provide for the first four
grades of basic education, but.gradually shift to grades 5 to 8. They would
also exercise supervisory functions over satellite schools. All classrooms
would be constructed to accommodate 80 students in two daily shifts. One-
third of the 390 single classroom schools would replace existing inadequate
facilities. The net increase in school places would be about 40,000. With a
two-shift use of the remaining 451 existing schools, a total of about 67,000
school places would exist, equivalent to 41% of the school age population
projected for 1985. The existing curriculum and calendar would be adjusted
to the educational needs of the rural population on the basis of an on-going
survey. Some 780 new teachers would be required in the satellite schools
and another 312 for the central NUAR schools. A two-stage training program
started in August 1980 is expected to reach some 840 teachers in the sub-
project area by February 1982 (out of a total staff requirement of about
2,000). Complementary training on teaching methodology of the new curriculum
would be financed by the project, together with the purchase of textbooks and
teachers guides. Operational responsibility for these schools would then remain
with the municipalities. Teacher salaries and other expenditures would be met
from Territory funds through the Secretariat of Education. Assurances were
received from the Territorial Government that it would (a) provide adequate
funding for teacher salaries; and (b) enter into agreements, before March 31,
1982, with each municipality in the subproject area, defining their respective
responsibilities in the implementation of the education component of the sub-
project.
4.14      Subproject Management.  The project would finance incremental
salaries and operating costs incurred by CODARON and the Rondonia Secretariat
of Planning (SEPLAN-RO) during the project implementation period in the
management of the Settlement Consolidation subproject. Funds would also be
made available for the monitoring of all subproject activities.
4.15      Parallel Activities.  On-farm credit would be provided principally
from the existing special lines of the Rubber Development Program (PROBOR)
for rubber, IBC for coffee and POLAMAZONIA, Selective Applications Program
(PROASE) and other BB or BASA investment lines for cocoa, livestock and farm
equipment, in parallel to the project. Standard credit lines of BB and BASA
would be available for annual crops and maintenance of perennials. Consider-
ing the high level of subsidies incorporated (interest rates now from 12 to
35%, with inflation in 1980 over 100%), credit would not be subject to

access to their farms, since this is, among other things, an obvious pre-
condition for reasonable marketing of their produce as well as a pre-
