- 21 -
project financing. Access to adequate credit remains, however, a fundamental
element of the development effort in the subproject area. On-farm invest-
ment credit of the order of US$38 million would be provided for a total of
some 18,200 small farmers (some getting more than one type of financing) to
help finance, among other things, the planting of coffee (some 6,600 loans),
cocoa (3,000 loans) and rubber (2,000 loans). Credit would also be utilized
for the purchase of a few cattle, pigs, chickens and on-farm equipment for the
proposed mixed farming systems. Seasonal credit would be provided to the same
producers, with priority to some 6,600 sharecroppers, for subsistence crops
during the initial years of tree crop establishment. BB and BASA would process
credit requests on the basis of applications approved by ASTER-RO. Estimated
seasonal credit requirements would reach about US$24.8 million by year 5 of
the project. Assurances were received from the Federal Government that
adequate working capital and investment credit would be made available in a
timely and simplified manner to project beneficiaries, with priority to
small farmers borrowing for the first time for perennial crop establishment.
4.16      The Secretariat of Agriculture of Rondonia and the Ministry of
Agriculture have developed a five-year annual crops seed production program,
which would aim to create within the territory the production capability of
certified seeds. That program would finance the installation of three seed
processing and storage units and the establishment of a coordinating committee
for the production of improved seeds and planting material and the training of
technicians. It would aim to produce about 3,000 tons/year of certified seed.
EMBRAPA would be responsible for the provision of selected basic seeds, and
ASTER-RO would train farmers who wish to be qualified as seed producers. The
total program costs of about US$1 million would be jointly financed by the
Territory and the Federal Government, with program implementation beginning
in 1981. The Government plan to improve seed supply (as defined by the Ministry
of Agriculture) would be fully executed in Rondonia before December 31, 1985,
and UEPAT would supply sufficient basic seeds for the production of improved
seeds in the project area. ASTER-RO and UEPAT would take such steps as may
be necessary to assure that prior to September 30, each year, they agree on
basic seeds requirements for the following crop-year.
4.17      As a continuation to the IBDF/EMBRAPA/FAO Tapajos National Forest
Project, feasibility studies would be conducted to study the composition of
the forest being cleared, identify trees with commercial value considering
transport costs to distant markets and determine the most efficient salvage
logging means to utilize trees of commercial value from the some one million
ha of forest expected to be converted to agricultural production within the
next ten years in the Settlement Consolidation subproject area (see Annex 6). I/
The FAO Technical Cooperation Program is expected to provide to IBDF a team of
eight consultants for that purpose, including specialists in tropical salvage
logging systems, forest industries, forest energy systems, forest revenue
systems, forest products marketing, forest economics, forestry extension and
agro-forestry, for periods of about two months each. Salvage logging
1/   Other commercial forestry activities in the Northwest region are discussed
in para 4.20.



- 22 -
studies would be completed by IBDF and their conclusions and recommendations
presented to the Bank before December 31, 1982. IBDF would then take the
necessary measures to put into effect such recommendations.
4e18      INCRA's on-going land titling program in the subproject area is
nearing completion. Assurances were received from the Federal Government that
INCRA would have provided all land-owners within the extended areas of the
five subproject settlements with definitive titles by March 31, 1982. Farmers
-who have received definitive titles are considered to have been "emancipated"
by INCRA. In principle, they are then free to sell part or all of their plot
on condition that each resulting holding maintain half of its area in forest
reserve, and that none of the resulting holdings be smaller than the official
INCRA "module," which is established at 30 ha in the region of the subproject.
The requirement to keep 50% of individual holdings in forest reserve has
proven difficult to enforce in other settlement areas. Also, this regulation
(though the aggregate objective is sound) results in higher access costs per
family settled or hectare planted because of the dispersion of planted areas.
Further, it may not necessarily ensure the preservation of biological diversity
as well as could be achieved within agglomerated reserves. INCRA will therefore
study the legal, technical and environmental feasibility of (a) authorizing
utilization of individual forest reserves for small farming purposes where
soils are proven to be suitable for crop production; and (b) where this proves
feasible, simultaneously establishing compensatory agglomerated forest reserves
elsewhere.  Such a study would be completed by INCRA and presented to the Bank
for review prior to June 30, 1982 and the recommendations of that study would
be carried out as appropriate promptly thereafter.
Environmental Protection
4.19      Zoning.  Zoning responsibilities in the Amazon region are shared
between various federal and state or territorial institutions. Detailed land
capability information on which to base future use planning and an improved
overall zoning of the Northwest region would be developed gradually, as the
combined result of a variety of actions. Some of these actions are already
undertaken outside the POLONOROESTE program, such as (i) existing Radambrasil
information at a 1:1,000,000 scale; and (ii) a soils and agricultural aptitude
survey at 1:250,000 scale contracted out by the Government of Rondonia to
EMBRAPA, for the whole territory, due to be gradually completed by 1984.
Other actions are being undertaken within the POLONOROESTE program, such as
(i) more detailed studies at a 1:50,000 scale contracted out by INCRA on
limited areas tentatively identified as suitable for agriculture, as a basis
for infrastructure and land use planning and individual plot allocations in
future settlements (see para 4.26); and (ii) the identification and demarcation
of Amerindian reserves (see para 6.03). Land capability knowledge at a scale
useful for on-farm planning, would be further developed by several distinct
approaches. Extension workers would be trained to develop empirical knowledge
to help farmers in their decisions, under the general extension program
discussed in para 4.09. A soils analysis laboratory would be maintained by
EMBRAPA to support this effort. At the same time, funds would be allocated
within the general EMBRAPA research program (see para 4.10) to provide for
some six months of consultancy services to collect all available information
and initiate land capability mapping. These operational zoning measures would
be complemented within the environmental protection subproject by several
special steps regarding the identification, establishment and protection of



- 23 -
forest reserves. Adequately implemented, these various measures would con-
siderably improve the land use planning of areas developed under this project.
The elaboration of similar measures would also be sought in future settlement
and rural development projects. In view, more particularly, of areas remain-
ing outside such specific projects, the Federal Government and the Territorial
Government have agreed to take all necessary measures to discourage the
agricultural exploitation of areas recognized as unsuitable for agricultural
development or of yet unproven suitability and to prevent the occupation of
areas which have been legally defined as reserves.
4.20      National Forests.  Proposals for setting up a national forest where
sustained commercial operations could be undertaken are still at an early stage
of preparation (see Annex 7, paras 5 and 6). A number of actions are required
prior to initiating corresponding investments in forestry production, research,
transportation, processing and marketing infrastructure. A forestry development
planning team has been set up within IBDF for initial research and field
studies in collaboration with INCRA to locate national forest sites (on future
agglomerated individual 50% reserves, on independent areas for leasing to
large agro-industries or on independent areas to promote forestry settlements
similar to agricultural settlements). The Fionas area, of some 300,000 ha,
located east of the Guapore Biological Reserve has been identified for purposes
of forestry development. IBDF will now proceed with (a) preliminary demarcation
and inventory; and (b) preparation of detailed plans for infrastructure and
management of a forestry development project, starting, if necessary, with a
pilot operation, with related financial and economic analyses. Actuai invest-
ments in production and support infrastructure would be the subject of a
separate project, while the subproject proposed here would finance the inputs
necessary to implement the preparatory phases, i.e. mainly consultant services,
transport and mapping costs, demarcation and protection of potential national
forest areas from encroachment. The Federal Government would, by March 31, 1982,
in consultation with the Bank, and through the Ministries of Agriculture and of
Interior, assign to agencies of these ministries specific responsibilities for
land use planning and for the identification, planning, financing and execution
of national forest projects. IBDF would set up its forestry development planning
team prior to March 31, 1982. It would also (a) provide to the Bank adequate
detailed terms of reference for the planning studies prior to June 30, 1982; and
(b) complete those studies prior to June 30, 1983. The Federal Government would
subsequently take all the necessary steps to implement appropriate controlled
forestry development plans.
4.21      Forestry Control Posts and Regional Strengthening of IBDF.  The
project would finance the reinforcement of the Porto Velho and Cuiaba offices
of IBDF, together with the establishment of a nursery at each of these locations.
Four new forest products control posts would be established in Pimenta Bueno
and Ji-Parana in Rondonia and Aripuana and Vila Bela in Mato Grosso, bringing
the total to seven, including existing posts in Vilhena (Rondonia) and Barra
do Bugres and Caceres (Mato Grosso), which would also be strengthened. Each
unit would be provided with offices, staff residences vehicles and equipment.
These control posts would monitor shipment of wood products, supervise forestry
extension and provide administrative assistance to existing and potential
forestry operators. Assurances were received from the Federal Government that
all seven IBDF forestry control posts in the Northwest region would be fully
equipped and operational by December 31, 1982.



- 24 -
4.22      Natural Reserves.  The project would help establish and protect
the Pacaas Novos National Park and the Guapore and Jaru Biological Reserves.
For the Pacaas Novos National Park, the project would build and equip central
administrative and residential buildings, six control posts and four simple
landing strips. It would provide for the purchase of transport and other
equipment, including a light, highwing, four seater airplane for monitoring
purposes. In addition, the project would finance personnel and operating
costs during five years and aerial photography of the Park. Since access
to part of the Park area has been interdicted by FUNAI until uru-eu-wau-wau
Indians dwelling in the area have been adequately contacted, prior to initiating
works in the Pacaas Novos National Park, IBDF and FUNAI will enter into an
agreement (a) spelling out arrangements to ensure collaboration between FUNAI
and IBDF in the protection of the Park and interdicted areas; (b) specifying
the IBDF works to be carried out in specific locations of the Park with the
agreement of FUNAI; and (c) defining procedures for FUNAI staff to accompany
IBDF workers where required. With the exclusion of the airplane, similar
inputs would be provided for the surveillance of the Guapore and Jaru Bio-
logical Reserves. Assurances were received from the Federal Government that
(a) the Guapore Biological Reserve would be defined and legally established no
later than March 31, 1982; and (b) IBDF would present detailed engineering
designs and plans for each reserve satisfactory to the Bank before the respec-
tive works are initiated.
4.23      Ecological Stations.  The project would assist SEMA in the estab-
lishment or extension of four ecological stations. In each ecological station,
SEMA would install the infrastructure and oversee its use by universities for
specific research programs agreed with SEMA. One of the stations, Ique, about
200,000 ha in Aripuana (Mato Grosso), is outside the official boundaries of
the Northwest region but is very useful for the knowledge of the region. A
second station, Serra das Araras (about 24,800 ha), is strategically located
in a forest/savannah transition area between Barra do Bugres and Caceres in
Mato Grosso. Infrastructure of the third station at Taiama (12,000 ha in the
Pantanal section of Caceres, Mato Grosso) would be completed by the subproject.
The fourth station (Cunia) lies on the Madeira river north of Porto Velho and
gives access to purely Amazonian landscape.  It includes some inundated land
as well as forest on "terra firme." Its definitive size (of the order of
100,000 ha) has recently been negotiated between SEMA, INCRA and the Govern-
ment of Rondonia. Costs of the four stations were estimated on the basis of
similar stations built by SEMA elsewhere in Brazil, adjusted for unit costs
prevailing in the region. A 20% physical contingency has been added to
accommodate possible design changes. Further details on the ecological
stations are provided in Annex 7, paras 13 through 17.  Assurances were
received from the Federal Government that the geographical boundaries of the
Cunia ecological station would be delimited by no later than March 31, 1982
and the station legally established thereafter. SEMA would present detailed
engineering designs and plans for each of the four ecological stations of
Ique, Serra das Araras, Taiama and Cunia, satisfactory to the Bank, before
any of the respective works are initiated.



- 25 -
Ecological Research
4.24      The Ecological Research subproject is to be carried out under the
supervision of the National Research Council (CNPq), which would subcontract
the implementation of various components to selected research institutions
and universities, preferably located in or near the program area. The research
would include (a) establishment of a hydrometeorology and biogeochemistry
monitoring system in Rondonia, conforming to national standards; (b) develop-
ment of a system for the inventory and collection of fauna and flora of
Rondonia; and (c) small-scale research projects on other selected topics as
may be agreed, possibly including research on the minimum size of eco-systems
and research on technologies appropriate to the region. Further details of
these research lines, which are subject to further revisions on the basis of
the final proposals of the institutions to be involved, are provided in
Annex 7, paras 21 through 24. CNPq would prepare by March 31, 1982 a detailed
ecological research program satisfactory to the Bank, including terms of
reference, budgets and implementation arrangements, and would promptly there-
after, assign implementation responsibilities satisfactory to the Bank based
on the detailed research proposals, including arrangements for CNPq to publish
by June 30 of each year a progress report setting forth the findings and
recommendations of research carried out under this program during the preceding
POLONOROESTE fiscal year.
Land Tenure Regularization in Mato Grosso
4.25      A 1979 survey showed that more than half of the farmers in the
proposed Northwest II - Mato Grosso Rural Development project area who were
eligible for legal title to their land had not yet received such title. Where
titles exist, furthermore, they often appear to be incorrect, and sometimes,
for historical reasons, more than one title exists for the same plot of land.
Inadequate titling has resulted in serious land disputes in areas of greater
population density and deprives the farmers of the incentive to carry-out
on-farm investment and of the collateral they often need to obtain investment
credit. It thus fosters shifting cultivation and other damaging forms of land
use. Land tenure regularization and titling would be implemented by the Land
Resources Department of INCRA and would cover all major areas of settlement of
Mato Grosso municipalities within the Northwest region, i.e. the nine municipal-
ities within the area of the proposed Northwest Phase II Mato Grosso Rural
Development Project (see para. 3.01) plus settlements along the Guapore Valley
(Pontes e Lacerda and Vila Bela). The project would provide (a) discrimi-
nation (identification of legal claims) of some 5.3 million ha; (b) demarcation
(topographical surveys and physical demarcation) of an estimated 600,000 ha
belonging to farmers owning more than 2 ha but less than 100 ha each; and (c)
titling of demarcated properties. The project would also finance preliminary
requirements such as cadastral micro-films and training of INCRA staff in Mato
Grosso. According to existing INCRA legislation, the costs of demarcation (to
be contracted by INCRA with local surveying firms) and titling is to be
financed by the landowners, but owners with less than 100 ha would be allowed
to spread their payments over five years. The project would therefore
finance the initial outlay of INCRA. Land regularization in Mato Grosso would
require three years to be completed. Geographical priorities would be estab-
lished in accordance with the requirements of the Mato Grosso Rural Development



- 26 -
project, for which land tenure regularization is considered to be a prerequisite
to active farmer participation. Small farmers concerned by the land tenure
regularization operations would be provided legal assistance throughout the
implementation of this component by lawyers to be employed for that purpose by
INCRA. INCRA would (a) establish, and provide adequate funds and staff for,
its Vila Bela Land Tenure project and complementary field units at Pontes e
Lacerda, Mirassol d'Oeste and Barra do Bugres by March 31, 1982; and (b) initiate
immediately titling procedures to provide owners of more than 2 ha in the Mato
Grosso Rural Development project area with definitive titles as soon as
demarcation of their plot is completed.
Soil Surveys for Future Settlement Areas
4.26      As a basis for its planning of new settlement in Rondonia and
Mato Grosso, the colonization division of INCRA has contracted out detailed
soil surveys (at 1:50,000 scale) of 70,000 ha in the Urupa area, south of Ouro
Preto, and 100,000 ha in the Machadinho region, north of Ariquemes, where
initial settlers of new settlement projects (Phase Three of POLONOROESTE)
could be established. As these pre-settlement surveys represent a key facet
of the underlying POLONOROESTE concept of rational land use planning and
zoning, the present project would finance these surveys, which are already
under way. Surveys of areas to be settled in subsequent years of the INCRA
settlement project would be financed within that same project.
Program Coordination, Monitoring and Studies
4.27      The project would finance all equipment and operating costs during
the project implementation period of a unit to be set up within SUDECO
for the specific purpose of coordinating all activities related to the North-
west program (see para 6.02). The project would also help strengthen local
SUDECO offices within the program area. Financing for the Rondonia Program
Coordination Unit is provided under the Settlement Consolidation subproject.
Similarly, the Mato Grosso Coordination Unit would be financed under the Phase
II Mato Grosso Rural Development project. Funds would be made available for
monitoring overall program progress, mid-term and final impact evaluation
surveys, ad-hoc studies on issues arising during and affecting project imple-
mentation, and general studies, including studies oriented to the identification
of future projects relevant to the development of the program area. The terms
of reference for special studies contracted by SUDECO under project funding
would have to be satisfactory to the Bank. Their results would be promptly
furnished to the Bank.



V. PROJECT COSTS AND FINANCING
A. Consolidated Project Costs
5.01      The total project cost over the five-year implementation period
(1981-1985) is estimated at US$199.3 million, which includes physical contin-
gencies equivalent to 11.9% 1/ of the baseline costs, and price contingencies
equivalent to 31.5% of baseline costs plus physical contingencies. 2/ Base-
line costs, estimated at January 1981 prices using the prevailing exchange
rate of US$1 = Cr$ 68.1, reflect the high costs of equipment and construction
material in Rondonia resulting from difficult transport and irregular supply.
Detailed designs would not be necessary for the relatively simple access roads
and buildings provided by the project. Corresponding cost estimates are based
on recent tenders for construction of similar standards in the project area.
The estimated foreign exchange component is US$42.7 million, or about 21.4% of
total project costs. Project costs include about US$10.0 million of readily
identifiable taxes. The project cost summary follows, and the yearly phasing
is given in Table 2 of Annex 5.
B. Financing
5.02      The proposed Bank loan of US$67 million would finance 35% of project
costs, net of taxes. The balance of project financing would be provided by
the Government, in part through special programs funds and the remainder
through the regular budgets of the agencies and local Governments involved.
The Federal and Territorial Governments have agreed to provide adequate funds,
staff and materials in a timely manner to appropriately maintain and operate
all project-developed infrastructure and services. Agricultural credit would
be provided directly by local banks, through existing credit lines. The Bank
loan would be for 15 years, including a three-year grace period. The Federa-
tive Republic of Brazil would be the borrower and would assume the foreign
exchange risk.
1/   Physical contingencies for the Settlement Consolidation subproject
average 11% of that subproject's baseline costs (15% for access roads;
10% for NUARs, warehouses, education and subproject administration; 7%
for extension, research and farmer organization). For the environmental
protection and ecological research subprojects, which require some
further detailed planning, physical contingencies of 20% were applied.
10% physical contingencies were applied to the remaining components of
the project.
21   Price contingencies were calculated in US dollars, at 9% in 1981,
8.5% in 1982, and 7.5% in 1983, 1984 and 1985 for all project costs.
They assume that the exchange rate of the Brazilian cruzeiro will be
periodically adjusted to compensate for any domestic inflation
above world inflation rates.

C. Procurement
5.03      The project would involve numerous, individually relatively small
items of construction and purchases of equipment or machinery in quite widely
dispersed areas. Farm access roads and civil works related to the rural service
centers and other project components would be built mainly through relatively
small contracts adjusted to the capacity of regional enterprises. Force
account work would be allowed for minor works in more isolated areas. Grain
stores would be built by CIBRAZEM using present contracting procedures, with
bidding advertised in Brazil. Foreign bidders would not be excluded but are
not likely to be interested given the remote locations. It is not likely that
foreign or even major national companies would be interested in tendering for
other construction or supply contracts. Works would be let out competitively
to local contractors by established Government procedures, which have been
reviewed and found satisfactory. Vehicles, machinery and equipment, acquired
by a variety of agencies over the project execution period, would also be
bought through competitive bidding advertized in Brazil, following existing
Government procedures which are satisfactory.
D. Disbursement
5.04      The proceeds of the Bank loan would be disbursed on the basis of a
rate of 55% of expenditures for each component until March 31, 1983, and
thereafter at such a rate that results in an average disbursement rate equal
to 33% of all expenditures. Disbursements would be made against Statements of
Expenditures prepared by the implementing agencies, certified by them--with
the exception of expenditures incurred by territorial agencies, which would be
certified by the Project Coordination Unit established within SEPLAN-RO--and
submitted to the Bank through the Central Bank. Supporting documentation for
minor civil works by force account and for operational expenses would not be
submitted to the Bank, but would be retained by the agency responsible for
their certification and made available for inspection by the Bank during
project supervision missions. Standard documentation covering civil works,
vehicles, equipment and technical assistance would be submitted to the Bank.
Disbursements are expected to occur over a period of about five years. The
closing date of the loan would be December 31, 1986.
5.05      Retroactive financing of up to US$5.5 million is recommended for
the costs of (a) land regularization surveys initiated in Mato Grosso after
May 31, 1981; (b) land capability surveys in areas of future INCRA settle-
ments, initiated after June 30, 1980; and (c) all Rondonia Settlement Con-
solidation subproject activities implemented after May 31, 1981. The
estimated schedule of disbursement is given in Table 3 of Annex 5.
5.06      In order to reduce the interval during which the Government would
finance the Bank's share of project cost with its own resources, a Special
Account would be opened in the Central Bank with an initial deposit of up to
US$4.0 million. The Government may request the Bank to make advance payments
in dollars from the loan account into this Special Account. Withdrawals
in cruzeiros from the Special Account would be made on the basis of the
statements of expenditures forwarded to the Central Bank. Conversion from
dollars into cruzeiros would be made at the exchange rate that prevailed on
the date the expenditures were made. The Central Bank would send the state-
ments to the Bank, which would then replenish the Special Account.



- 30 -
VI. PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION
A. Central Program Administration and Coordination
General
6.01      The overall administration and coordination of the proposed North-
west program and first phase group of projects would be within the legal and
organizational framework applicable to Brazil's other special programs for
regional development. Presidential Decree No. 83.436 of May 10, 1979 gives
the responsibility for planning, coordinating, monitoring and evaluating these
programs to regional superintendencies attached to the Ministry of Interior.
The Secretariat of Planning retains, however, a major role in the definition
of financing levels of all special programs. Inter-Ministerial Orders
(Portarias) 90 and 91 of May 27, 1980 define (a) the modalities of collabora-
tion between the Ministry of Interior, other Ministries and the State Govern-
ments and agencies involved in the special programs; and (b) programming,
budgeting and fund disbursement procedures. A complete list of agencies
involved in program implementation is given in Annex 2. Decree No. 86029
of May 27, 1981 formally established the POLONOROESTE program (discussed in
Chapter II) and an estimated budget. Adjustments which may be required to
that budget would be implemented through the annual rebudgeting process.
Failure to comply with any covenants in agreements between the Government and
the Bank on the proposed Northwest I-Highways and Northwest I-Health loans
would represent non-compliance with the agreements for this proposed loan,
pursuant to the Bank's general conditions.
The Superintendency for Economic Development in the Center-West Region (SUDECO)
6.02      Responsibility for the administration of the proposed Northwest program
has been entrusted to SUDECO. An Interministerial Coordination Group (Grupo
de Articulacao Interministerial), in which the key ministries concerned would be
represented along with the Ministry of Interior and the Planning Secretariat,
set up under Portaria 91, would facilitate coordination among the various
federal agencies and help jointly resolve issues arising out of the implementa-
tion of the program. SUDECO would establish a POLONOROESTE General Coordination
TUnit under its Deputy Superintendency of Planning, but also closely linked to the
Deputy Superintendency of Operations. The Coordination Unit would be divided
into two sections, one responsible for the harmonization of program activities,
by establishing liaison with federal and state level agencies (the latter
through SUDECO offices in Porto Velho and Cuiaba); the other in charge of
planning (i.e. consolidating proposals of various implementing agencies for
the), monitoring and evaluation of project progress and impact, and special
studies. Under the General Coordinator, the liaison section would have a
minimum staff of five professionals, and the Monitoring and Evaluation Section
would have seven professionals. A POLONOROESTE Coordinator has been appointed
within SUDECO, with qualifications, experience and employment conditions
satisfactory to the Bank.  SUDECO has agreed to (a) appoint the two Section
Chiefs of the POLONOROESTE program coordination unit, with qualifications,
experience and employment conditions satisfactory to the Bank and complete the
establishment of the POLONOROESTE program coordination unit by March 31, 1982;
(b) maintain this unit fully staffed at all times with personnel whose
qualifications, experience and conditions of employment are satisfactory to



- 31 -
the Bank; and (c) provide to the Bank the proposed budget for the POLONOROESTE
program by component, for the following year, by October 31 of each year, and
finalized budgetary allocations for the program each year as soon as that budget
is formally approved.
Amerindian Protection
6.03      Rapid occupation and development is bound to place increasing pres-
sure on the Amerindian communities in the Program area because of competition
for their land and risk of contagious diseases to which the Amerindian popu-
lation is not immune. In the very worst case, the cultural or even physical
extinction of Amerindian groups living in the area could be feared if devel-
opment were not carefully controlled. Government authorities have, therefore,
commited themselves, as part of the POLONOROESTE program, to strengthening
their capacity to protect the indigenous population in this frontier area.
FUNAI will give priority attention to the protection of Amerindian lands and
of their health in accordance with the special project prepared by FUNAI with
the concurrence of the Bank (see Annex 8). It will strengthen its operations
in the Northwest region by upgrading its regional administrative infrastruc-
ture, establishing and equipping new Amerindian posts and increasing and
training its field staff. Planning is to be decentralized for more efficient
field level involvement. Central coordination and synchronization with
POLONOROESTE activities is to be provided by a special coordinator who has
already been appointed. These activities would be financed entirely by the
Government, which would closely monitor and periodically review, with the
Bank, the Amerindian protection activities. For the protection of designated
indigenous areas, whether interdicted, delineated or fully demarcated, FUNAI
is to (a) have illegal trespassers and occupants evicted; and (b) demarcate
Amerindian areas as scheduled under its Special Project for indigenous com-
munities in the area of influence of BR-364 and, where necessary, upgrade
existing demarcation lines. Health care would be upgraded by improving and
expanding the operations of existing mobile health units and establishing
new health posts in the Guapore Valley. Back-up support would be provided
by the Vilhena hospital. FUNAI would provide (a) a full immunization coverage;
and (b) intensified assistance in cases of outbreaks of communicable diseases.
FUNAI would also maintain adequate medical supplies at all Indian Posts.
Further lines of action would seek to promote education and economic develop-
ment activities. FUNAI and DNER have entered into arrangements enabling them
to cooperate towards the protection of the Amerindian communities against
possible negative effects associated with the highway construction works.
FUNAI has received necessary funds for initiating planned Amerindian protection
measures in the POLONOROESTE area. Assurances were received that the Federal
Government would take all necessary steps to promptly implement the special
project for protecting the interests of the Amerindian communities located in
the program area, in phase with other program components so as to ensure that
key protective measures accompany or, where necessary, precede the initiation
of the relevant development activities. To this effect, the Federal Government
would (a) ensure timely provision of appropriate funds, staff and other



- 32 -
resources, authorizations and permits required for the implementation of the
protection measures; (b) upgrade the organization and operations of its
institutions entrusted with implementation of the program; (c) provide the
Bank a reasonable opportunity to periodically review in the field progress on
the implementation of these protective measures; and (d) present to the Bank
such information as the Bank may require on the carrying out an impact of the
protection program. It is FUNAI's intention to enter into a contract by
May 31, 1982 with a Brazilian institution or experts to assist them in their
monitoring and evaluation activities related to the Special Project. For the
same purposes, legal acts (portarias) delimiting all Amerindian areas in
the Guapore Valley have been executed, and additional personnel, equipment
and operating resources required by FUNAI have been provided in the program
area.
B. Settlement Consolidation Subproject Management
Program Coordination at the Territory Level
6.04      The Government of the Territory of Rondonia would have overall
responsibility for the execution of the Settlement Consolidation subproject.
A Territorial Directing Council, chaired by the Governor of Rondonia and
comprising high-level representatives of the federal ministries involved
through their agencies in the Northwest program, key state secretariats and
CODARON, would establish policies and guidelines, approve annual plans and
provide political coordination for POLONOROESTE operations within the Terri-
tory of Rondonia. Federal funds for territorial secretariats and agencies
would be channelled through SEPLAN-RO, which would coordinate the participa-
tion of territorial agencies involved in the POLONOROESTE Program in Rondonia,
enter into agreements ("convenios") with federal agencies, establishing their
participation under the program, and monitor progress in program implementa-
tion in the Territory. To this purpose, SEPLAN-RO has established a special
Program Coordination Unit, responding to the Territorial Directing Council.
(Funds for federal agricultural agencies operating within the subproject
area, such as IBDF, INCRA, EMBRAPA and CIBRAZEM will be channelled directly to
them from federal sources.) The Territorial Program Coordination Unit would
(a) provide liaison with SUDECO; (b) coordinate preparation of annual plans
for POLONOROESTE activities undertaken by various participating agencies
within the Territory; (c) organize personnel training; (d) maintain the
combined financial records and control of program expenditures in the Terri-
tory; and (e) undertake the overall monitoring and evaluation of these activi-
ties, complemented by special studies, as required. The Unit would consist of
an Administrative Nucleus and a Technical Support Nucleus. The Administrative
Nucleus would ensure the budgetary supervision and consolidation of project
accounts. The Technical Support Nucleus would ensure technical execution
coordination of various territorial agencies participating in the program,
through three subunits dealing respectively with agriculture, physical
infrastructure and social sector activities. The Agricultural Planning
Commission (CEPA) would handle for CODARON and the Program Coordination Unit



- 33 -
the monitoring of the agricultural components of the Rondonia Settlement
Consolidation subproject. A project coordination and management chart is
given in Annex 3.
The Agricultural Development Company of Rondonia
6.05      The main responsibility for the implementation of the Settlement
Consolidation subproject will be with the Agricultural Development Company
of Rondonia (CODARON: Companhia de Desenvolvimento Agricola de Rondonia).
CODARON is a public corporation created by Law No. 6805, dated July 7, 1980,
which became formally operational on January 27, 1981. Its creation was
closely linked to the establishment of the POLONOROESTE program. It is
planned that CODARON will take over most activities previously undertaken
by the Secretariat of Agriculture of Rondonia. Most of the 370 staff members
of the Secretariat are expected to be transferred to CODARON. The former
Secretary of Agriculture has been appointed as President of CODARON. The
corporate structure of CODARON is expected to considerably simplify con-
tracting, recruitment and other operational procedures, thus -- it is hoped --
increasing the administrative efficiency in handling the complex activities of
the POLONOROESTE program.
6.06      The actual implementation and local coordination of rural develop-
ment activities would be entrusted to regional units ("gerencias"), one to
be established in each municipality. CODARON-appointed managers of the
39 NUARs would respond directly to these regional units. Thus, the central
structure of CODARON would be kept lightly staffed and flexible, with a core
staff (five "coordinators") in the Directorate of Operations providing planning,
monitoring and advisory services to the regional units, mainly through ad-hoc
task forces. This core staff would include a Coordinator of Rural Organization,
who would be responsible for overseeing the planning and general supervision
of all investment activities carried out by CODARON under the settlement
consolidation subproject, i.e., mainly the construction of access roads and
NUAR infrastructure (including schools and, under the Northwest health project,
health posts). A Coordinator of Agricultural Production would ensure the
overall coordination of all services provided to farmers by local branches of
participating agencies, such as extension, credit and storage. He would also
organize and generally supervise the monitoring of on-farm results of the
development effort. A Marketing Coordinotor would be responsible for ensuring
commercial arrangements with controlled profit margins for the timely availability
of farm inputs such as improved seeds (produced by private farmers), seedlings,
fertilizers and herbicides. The other two coordinators (Special Projects and
Internal Productive Units) would not be directly involved in the Rondonia settle-
ment consolidation subproject. Preparation and financial supervision of civil
works and other procurement contracts would be done by CODARON's Directorate of
Administration and Finances. The regional CODARON units and NUAR managers would
provide the on-site technical supervision of contractors, organize community labor
and coordinate the local activities of ASTER-RO, CIBRAZEM, INCRA and other agen-
cies dealing with agricultural production and marketing at the farm level.
They would also help, at the local level, synchronizing the activities of the
participating institutions, such as the municipal Secretariats of Education
and Health and social workers of the area. This will help ensure consistency
between NUAR construction schedules and related municipal responsibilities,
such as the recruitment and training of teachers and health staff. A super-
visor and eight subordinate technical and administrative staff would be
recruited at each NUAR for the operation and maintenance of the facilities.



- 34 -
6.07      The initial staff of CODARON will consist of the employees trans-
ferred from the Secretariat of Agriculture, mainly agronomists, extensionists,
surveyors, social workers and support personnel. While the present staff of
CODARON has some experience in agricultural development, both through their
prior activities within the Secretariat of Agriculture and their participation
in the preparation of this subproject, they will require further training,
especially at middle administrative and field technical levels. Furthermore,
CODARON is at present undergoing rapid changes, and the numbers of both
headquarters and field staff are expected to grow considerably during project
implementation. Except for the incremental field operation (e.g., NUAR level)
staff financed by the project, the staffing and funding of CODARON would be
the responsibility of the Territorial Government. CODARON has hired a Brazilian
consultancy firm, for a period of one year, with whom they have been preparing
the basic manuals of the institution (organization, accounting, procurement,
personnel, etc.). CODARON still needs to complete recruitment of qualified
staff for key positions in its planned organizational structure. Furthermore,
CODARON would require longer term assistance in refining and developing its
administrative and technical systems. Assurances were received from the
Territorial Government that CODARON would (a) prepare detailed terms of
reference for, and then appoint before March 31, 1982, (i) a chief project
planning and supervision officer within CODARON's advisory unit (assesoria)
for the Settlement Consolidation subproject; (ii) a rural organization coordi-
nator; (iii) an agricultural production coordinator; (iv) a marketing coordi-
nator; (v) a chief accountant; and (vi) the chief of the procurement and
contracting unit, all with terms of reference, qualifications, experience and
conditions of employment satisfactory to the Bank; and. (b) contract by March
31, 1982 the services of consultants with terms of reference, qualifications
and experience satisfactory to the Bank, to provide CODARON with administrative
and technical expertise particularly in accounting, procurement, access road
construction and subproject monitoring, for a period of about two years, to
be extended or redefined as appropriate, based on findings of the project's
mid-term review.
C.   Other Subprojects
Environmental Protection
6.08      The Brazilian Institute for Forest Development (IBDF).  IBDF is an
autonomous organ of the Ministry of Agriculture, established in 1967 and
charged with (a) formulation of forest policy; (b) directing the rational use
and conservation of renewable natural resources; and (c) forestry development.
Its functions are based on the comprehensive Forest Code of 1965 and Fauna
Protection Law of 1967. (A new, more specific Amazonian Forest Policy paper,
proposed by an inter-ministerial commission to help implement land use policies
in the Amazon region, is now also under consideration by the Government.)
Under its President, IBDF has five executive departments: Research; Reforesta-
tion; Forest Economics; National Parks and Reserves; Industry and Commerce.
There is an IBDF delegation in each state. IBDF receives technical assistance
from a resident FAO mission. The Special Programs Division of the Forest
Economics department is to coordinate IBDF's work with POLONOROESTE. The said
department also has a division of Remote Sensing and Monitoring which produces
maps out of landsat imagery, showing the extent of forest clearing and new



- 35 -
planting and identifying any significant invasions occurring on National Parks
and Reserves. Overall, IBDF's conservation program has 23 National Parks
(8.3 million ha) and 11 Biological Reserves (1.2 million ha). Ten of these
units, totalling 7 million ha, have been created within the past two years,
but IBDF does not yet have the necessary staff and financial resources to
administer them. In 1980, IBDF's budget totalled Cr$1.6 billion (or about
US$32 million), two-thirds of which was allocated to the coordination of the
forest development policy. IBDF staff (October 1980) totalled some 3,400,
including 500 professionals (of which 167 foresters and 83 agronomists),
1,400 technicians and 1,500 administrative personnel. Until recently, IBDF
was largely inactive in Rondonia and had only limited activities in Mato
Grosso. The POLONOROESTE program, and this project in particular, will
represent a major strengthening of IBDF work in the Northwest.
6.09      The IBDF National Parks and Reserves Department would be respon-
sible for the establishment, protection and operation of the National Park of
Pacaas Novos and the Biological Reserves of Jaru and Guapore, according to
procedures established for other parks and reserves in Brazil. Since the
Pacaas Novos park is located in an area where uncontacted Amerindian tribes
are believed to dwell, IBDF would maintain permanent liaison with FUNAI
throughout project implementation. The small plane to be acquired within the
Pacaas Novos investments would be available for the monitoring of all other
natural reserves in the Territory of Rondonia. Implementation responsibility
for the preparation of National Forest projects would be with the Department
of Forest Economics, where an ad-hoc working group would use satellite infor-
mation, aerial photographs, fly-overs and field visits to identify large areas
of prime forest land (in blocks of possibly one million ha each) fulfilling
criteria of forest types, commercial timber volumes, topography and accessi-
bility. INCRA would also be closely involved in the preparation of these
studies because of the possibility of establishing national forests within the
agglomerated legal reserves of future settlements or because national forests
could possibly become themselves the frame of some future settlements. Once
national forest areas identified, they would be declared legal forest reserves
and protected by ranger patrols.
6.10      The Special Secretariat for the Environment (SEMA).  SEMA is an
autonomous agency of the Ministry of Interior, created in 1973 to work in
the fields of environmental conservation and rational use of natural resources
(monitoring environmental changes, establishing norms, and promoting train-
ing and public education). SEMA has at present nine ecological research
stations, including two in the Northwest region to be improved under the
project. In addition, the project would help establish two new stations.
Fourteen universities have current research programs in these stations, with
research funds coming from the Ministry of Education and Special Regional
Programs. SEMA has a staff of about 170. The ecological stations are usually
run by one director plus three administrative or support staff.
Ecological Research Institutions
6.11      Scientific and technological research in Brazil has been coordinated
since 1979 by the National Research Council (CNPq) with headquarters in
Brasilia. Responsibility for the various ecological research work would be
contracted out by CNPq based on an evaluation of proposals it receives from
research institutions wishing to participate, priority being given to institu-
tions located within or near the program area. It is already known that



- 36 -
research in hydrometeorology and biogeochemical budgets in the Amazon basin
is part of the work program of the Center for Nuclear Research in Agriculture
(CENA), with headquarters in Piracicaba, Sao Paulo, and the National Institute
for Amazon Research (INPA), with headquarters in Manaus, which receives ancil-
lary support from a number of institutes and from EMBRAPA. These agencies
have considerable experience with hydrometeorological networks in the Amazon
basin. There would be obvious advantages in linking the proposed Rondonia
network to the existing grid. INPA is also involved in an on-going minimum
ecosystem experiment. Together with the Museo Goeldi, located in Belem, INPA
has undertaken some taxonomic surveys of the Amazon region. Some lines of
research could also be appropriate for the University of Mato Grosso, in
Cuiaba, where research capability nearest to the project area is to be found.
The University of Rondonia, in Porto Velho, is at a much earlier stage of
development but may also play some role in the ecological research program.
Land Tenure Regularization in Mato Grosso
6.12      Since the areas to be regularized fall within 100 km on either side
of a federal highway (BR-364) of the Amazon region and/or within 150 km of a
national border, INCRA is, by law, the only institution authorized to establish
or confirm ownership rights and issue land titles. It does so through its
Department of Land Resources. Discrimination of areas to be regularized in
Mato Grosso would be done by the staff of the three INCRA land tenure projects
(projetos fundiarios) of Diamantino, Caceres and Vila Bela (for the Guapore
Valley). The Diamantino and Caceres projects are already operational, with
a total staff of about 110, of which some 40 are of professional level.
Activities in the Diamantino and Caceres areas are to be initiated in mid-1981.
The Vila Bela project is expected to become operational before the end of
1981. Topographical survey companies to do the actual demarcation work are
available in the region. Titling would be provided as soon as demarcation
is completed.
Soil Surveys for Future Settlement Areas
6.13      Soil surveys for the initial settlements of the future INCRA
program are already under way in areas shown by less detailed surveys to have
promising agricultural potential. INCRA's Land Settlement Department has
contracted these surveys to EMBRAPA's soil surveys section for the Machadinho
area and to the Technological Center Foundation of Minas Gerais (CETEC), for
the Urupa area. In the Urupa area, which lies very close to existing settle-
ments, INCRA has taken measures to avoid its invasion by squatters prior to
the construction of the eventual infrastructure. These surveys are almost
completed, and will be used in the preparation of the proposed INCRA project
(Phase III of POLONOROESTE) to be presented shortly to the Bank for appraisal
of possible financing.
D. Implementation Schedule
6.14      A chronogram of the main project activities is given in Annex 1.
While investments on farm access roads and rural service centers are expected
to be completed within five years, most of the construction and equipment
investments related to environmental protection and ecological research are
expected to be achieved within the first two to three years.



- 37 -
E. Monitoring and Evaluation
The Northwest Program
6.15      The Monitoring and Evaluation Section (see para 6.02) of SUDECO
would comprise a coordinator, an economist, an agronomist, a civil engineer,
a sociologist and an ecologist. This section, assisted by independent
consultants acceptable to the Bank to be contracted by March 31, 1982
following Bank policies and procedures, would be mainly responsible for the
on-going evaluation of the entire program's progress on the basis of monitor-
ing data elaborated and compiled by each implementing agency of the
POLONORDESTE program. Furthermore, it will monitor planning, programming,
budgeting and coordination activities at the federal administration level and
inside SUDECO, contract out ad-hoc mid term and final impact studies and super-
vise their implementation. Monitoring results would be consolidated into
quarterly reports.
Specific Sub-Projects
6.16      For the Rondonia Settlement Consolidation subproject, the Coor-
dination Unit of SEPLAN-RO (see para 6.04) with assistance from the Rondonia
Agricultural Planning Commission (CEPA-RO) on agricultural components, would
monitor subproject implementation progress and its impact on the farmers and
the environment. The Coordination Unit would consolidate resulting data into
quarterly reports. Similar procedures would be established on the environmental
protection and ecological research subprojects by IBDF and SEMA for the former
and CNPq for the latter. Monitoring indicators will be further elaborated by
the respective institutions and translated into specific monitoring arrange-
ments and procedures. These procedures would be developed around an existing
project-based computerized data system (SAPE-GRAFF) already in use by most
Government agencies. This system follows up the physical and financial progress
of various project activities. It would be complemented to (a) expand monitoring
to cover the qualitative and quantitative impact of the project on farmer
income and development; and (b) develop a project evaluation methodology.
Various universities and consultancy firms have already been contacted for
this purpose by SUDECO and by CODARON. Each of the executing entities involved
in POLONORDESTE activities would establish and provide to the Bank through
SUDECO, prior to March 31, 1982, detailed monitoring plans and procedures
satisfactory to the Bank. SUDECO would compile all quarterly monitoring
reports for transmittal to the Bank promptly upon completion and provide to
the Bank, promptly upon their completion, (a) research and evaluation studies
carried out during project execution; (b) a mid-term evaluation, to be
implemented before June 30, 1984, of progress in POLONOROESTE program activities
up to March 31, 1984; and, (c) within six months of project completion, a
completion report evaluating the project's impact.
F.   Accounting and Auditing
6.17      Each of the participating agencies would maintain separate accounts
of its project expenditures, which would be audited annually by the Central



38 -
Secretariat of Internal Control (SECIN) of the Federal Secretariat of Planning
according to standard Government practice, which is satisfactory to the Bank.
SUDECO would maintain accounts of its own project expenditures for program
coordination as well as summary records of the project accounts of each
participating agency. Copies of the audited statements of project accounts
of the various participating agencies would be provided to the Bank through
SUDECO within six months of the end of the fiscal year of the POLONOROESTE
program. Not later than six months after the close of the fiscal year, SUDECO
would present to the Bank the results of an audit of the Statements of Expen-
ditures carried out by independent auditors acceptable to the Bank. This audit
report would convey the auditor's opinion (and comments as necessary) on the
methodology employed in the compilation of the Statements of Expenditures,
their accuracy, the relevance of supporting documents, eligibility for financ-
ing in terms of the project's legal agreements and the standard of record
keeping and internal controls related to the foregoing. The Special Account
would be audited by the Internal Auditing Department of the Central Bank.
VII. PRODUCTION, DEMAND, MARKETING AND PRICES
7.01      Chapters VII, VIII and most of IX deal exclusively with the Rondonia
Settlement Consolidation subproject, which is the directly production oriented
component of the project, and as such, most readily subject to quantifiable
financial and economic analysis.
A. Production
7.02      The Settlement Consolidation subproject would reinforce existing
Government programs already initiated in the project area to expand produc-
tion, principally of coffee, cocoa and rubber. Support would also be given to
improve the productivity, reduce losses and expand production of annual crops
such as rice, corn, beans and manioc, and to purchase and use small animals
and livestock fitting the typical farmer's production pattern and consumption
requirements.
7.03      Production increases in the subproject area are expected to result
from (a) increased production of 18,200 farmers who would directly receive
full project support and credit; (b) all other farmers in the subproject area
who would see their losses reduced due to better transport and marketing;
(c) increased production of annual crops by newly arriving sharecroppers or
laborers; and (d) an expansion, mainly of tree crops, in already developed
farms of the area, which have achieved the capacity to self-finance such
plantings (the latter two items not to be considered as direct benefits
of the project proposed for Bank financing). The reduction of post-harvest
losses would mostly affect the rice crop and is expected to amount to about
10% of actual production. The production increases of project beneficiaries
would be mainly due to (a) moderate increases in tree crop yields with some
expansion of tree crop areas; (b) an expansion of small animal production;
(c) a modest increase in average yields of annual crops due to improved techniques
(timing, plant spacing, weeding) and inputs (seeds, fertilizers, insecticides)
promoted under the technical assistance component; and (d) a reduction in rice
crop losses from an average of 30% to an average of 20%, due to improved on-farm



- 39 -
storage and drying, improved transportation and off-farm storage and drying
facilities. Yields assumed at full development are below levels already
attained by the most progressive farmers in the project area. They reflect
the mission's best estimates of what could be achieved on average, given the
level of project farmers' resources, managerial capacity and the availability
of improved Government services.
7.04      A summary of expected production increases in the Settlement Con-
solidation subproject area, derived from the four representative farm models
(discussed in Chapter VIII) is given in Table 5. These figures represent a
conservative estimate of the development to be achieved in the project area
under the project. The assumed expansion in tree crops under the project
is thought to be the minimum that moderately successful direct participants
would undertake. The more ambitious and resourceful beneficiaries (as in the
case of the already well established settlers who would not be the focus of
this project) would, in any case, continue to expand their stands of tree
crops. (Corresponding income was not considered in the economic analysis of
the project since it would not have been generated by the project.) The
estimated incremental production of annual crops is also conservative given
the fact that the rural population will probably continue to grow rapidly,
with new arrivals continuing to clear plots for cultivation of subsistence
crops while working on the tree crops of an established settler. Annual crops
would initially be intercropped under perennials, with a gradual shift towards
continuous cropping with adequate rotations, possibly including fallow periods.
Farmers are likely to prefer continuous cultivation to shifting cultivation
since the former can give higher returns per manday, require less total labor
and result in lower seasonal labor demand peaks. The adoption of continuous
cultivation would require, however, that adequate extension services, credit
and inputs 'especia'lly of improved seeds and essential fertilizers) be available.
Since the shift to continuous cultivation would be done over a five to six
years period, the project would have reasonable time to establish the necessary
conditions for its implementation (see Annex 4).
7.05      At estimated January 1981 farmgate prices, adjusted to take into
consideration improvements resulting from parallel investments in roads and
from market information services, directly project-generated incremental annual
production at full development (beyond year 15) is expected to amount to
US$143.5 million. Approximately 31% of this value would come from incremental
production of coffee, 25% from cocoa and 21% from rubber, with the remainder
being generated from various animal products (12%) and annual crops (11%).
B. Demand, Markets and Prices
7.06      Brazil produces 25% of the world's coffee.  High prices in 1979 have
been followed by lower price levels which are expected to persist in the medium
term in the light of world economic conditions and large stocks which resulted
from declines in consumption while world production was rising. The influence
of the severe frost of July 1981 in southern Brazil on world coffee supply and
prices has not yet been fully appraised. Some 26,800 ha are currently planted
in coffee in the Rondonia subproject area, about half of it planted in the
past three years, producing about 8,100 tons of green beans in 1980. Without
the project, the area planted in coffee might be expected to increase

to about 74,600 ha by 1986/87. The annual production from these plantings
would amount to about 78,700 tons by around 1995. With the project, the total
area planted in coffee would reach some 92,000 ha in the next five to seven
years, or about 17,400 ha more than without the project. The total increase
(i.e. that which would occur even without the project, together with the
increase directly facilitated by the project) in coffee plantings in the
subproject area, where climate and soil conditions are favorable to coffee,
represents only 8% of the 750,000 ha which would be planted nationwide within
the next five years, assuming a continuation of Brazil's general efforts to
replant 5% of the country's 3 million ha in coffee each year, gradually taking
coffee out of the frost-prone areas in southern Brazil states. Incremental
coffee production directly generated by the project (i.e. the difference
between "with" and "without project" production) by 1995, is expected to be of
the order of 24,200 tons of dry beans. This represents less than 2% of the
average Brazilian production during the last five years or less than 0.5% of
recent world production. The International Coffee Organization (ICO) foresees
a possible world oversupply of coffee by the end of the decade a possibility.
ICO reintroduced quotas on exports of coffee in October 1980 and has cautioned
against investment in projects designed to increase production significantly,
unless such increased production is due to a diversified pattern of agriculture
as part of an integrated rural development program. Production from such
programs should be offset by reduced production from marginal areas taken out
of coffee. The proposed project meets these criteria as the production of
coffee in the project area is part of rural development efforts oriented
towards relatively low-income farmers. Moreover, it is expected that the new
production will replace coffee from less suitable areas such as the "frost
belt" areas of Parana, where coffee production in declining. In the Northwest,
on the other hand, ecological considerations make tree crops particularly
suitable to protect soil resources, while other crop alternatives are limited.
Brazil is a major member of the ICO and has demonstrated its interest in an
orderly world coffee market and its support for the International Coffee
Agreement. To achieve this, coffee development is monitored, and the Govern-
ment makes use principally of price and credit mechanisms to contain or expand
coffee production as appropriate. Project returns are computed on the assump-
tion that the price of coffee would remain at current relatively low levels
for the next 10 years, as projected by the Bank. 1/
7.07      The Territory of Rondonia planting targets for cocoa and rubber were
established by the respective commodity institutions, based on studies of
international markets and national production levels. These targets, envisage
the establishment in Rondonia of 100,000 ha of cocoa and 35,000 ha of rubber
between 1981 and 1985. Expansion envisaged in the subproject area is well
within these limits (see Table 5), representing only 30% of the territorial
cocoa program and 57% of the rubber to be planted, including estimates of
spontaneous plantings by farmers who are not direct beneficiaries of the
project, whereas the subproject area contains half of Rondonia's rural popula-
tion and an even higher percentage of the soils adequate for these crops.
World cocoa production fluctuates widely; demand for cocoa is inelastic
and prices are very sensitive to changes in world production. Production is
increasing very rapidly in Brazil, Cameroon, Ivory Coast and Malaysia but has
declined in recent years in Ghana and Nigeria. The high world prices that
1/   See E.P.D.C.E. Memorandum:  Commodity Price Forecasts Updating, June 22,
1981, page 4.



42 
prevailed in 1977-1978 have declined to their lowest level since early 1976.
They are expected to remain at current low levels, in real terms, through the
1980's, while world production is expected to continue to increase. 1/ Brazil
has some 640,000 ha planted in cocoa, of which some 480,000 ha are in production.
With yields around 720 kg/ha of beans9 the 1981 production is estimated at
about 3409000 tons, (i.e., about one-fifth of world production and close to
that of the world's largest producer, Ivory Coast). Annual cocoa production
has been forecast to further rise to 450,000 t by 1985 and to 500,000 t by the
end of the decade, confirming Brazil as the world's largest cocoa producer.
Targets for expansion between 1976 and 1985 were 300,000 ha for planting and
150,000 ha for replanting. Of the 450,000 ha total, 245,000 ha would be
established between 1981 and 1985. Incremental cocoa production in the sub-
project area (22,500 tons by about 1995) would represent about 1.3% of present
world production and less than 5% of Brazil's planned production for the 1990s.
7.08      World demand for elastomers is increasing at a rate that cannot be
met by existing areas planted with rubber, nor by increased output from
existing synthetic rubber factories, which are furthermore affected by increas-
ing oil prices. Three major producers -- Malaysia, Indonesia and Thailand --
dominate the world natural rubber market, producing among them 80% of the
total. Of the three, only Thailand's exports show a rapidly rising trend.
About two-thirds of Brazil's 65,000 t natural rubber and one-fifth of its
180,000 t synthetic rubber requirements are imported. Yet, Brazil is consid-
ered to have a long term potential to become a net rubber exporter. Plans
embodied in the rubber development program (PROBOR I and II) aim at increasing
the area planted to rubber in Brazil by 120,000 ha within the next five years.
While current price incentives to local farmers will probably decrease as
imports are reduced, and thus the present difference (of the order of two to
one) between Brazilian and world natural rubber prices would narrow, inter-
national prices are expected to increase by about 10% over the next ten years,
which should permit the maintenance of an attractive price to the domestic
producer for the foreseeable future.
7.09      The Rondonia urban and rural population is expected to grow at a
faster rate than the area's production of most annual crops and animal pro-
ducts. Incremental production of these commodities would therefore be mainly
absorbed by local demand. Corn is consumed mostly by local livestock and
poultry. Beans and manioc (as flour) would be consumed on-farm or in the
neighboring urban centers and villages. Rice production is expected to reach
a surplus (net of on-farm consumption) of about 38,000 tons of paddy, which
would be consumed in urban centers in the territory and neighboring states.
It can be noted that incremental project production shown in Table 5 repre-
sents less than 1% of Brazilian output for annual crops (about 9% for coffee
and 13% for cocoa) shown in Table 3.  World prices for food crops in general
are expected to at least remain stable and possibly increase up to 30% for
rice and beans and 60% for corn over the next decade in real terms (see update
to Bank Report 814-80: Price Projections for Major Primary Commodities). This
is especially true for Brazil, where competition for suitable soils between
fooderops, commercial export crops and cultivation of sugarcane, manioc and
other products for fuel production is expected to move prices in an upward
trend. The main prices used in the financial and economic analyses of the
1/   See E.P.D.C.E. Memorandum:  Commodity Price Forecasts Updating, June 22,
1981, page 4.



- 43 -
Settlement Consolidation subproject are given in Annex 5, Table 4. Prices used
in the forestry and environmental protection sub-projects are shown in various
tables appended to Annex 7. Further cost data are available in various Staff
Working Papers.
C. Marketing Channels
7.10      Possibly the major constraint to agricultural development in the
region has been the difficulty of farmers' access to markets. Tremendous
premiums are paid, especially during the rainy season (November-May) when
rice is harvested, for transporting produce to the BR-364, where most of
the purchasing points are to be found. Losses due to spoilage and rotting
are very high (estimated, on the average, at 30% for rice). Purchasers
of produce are usually (a) trucker/intermediaries operating along BR-364;
(b) owners of intermediate processing facilities for rice, coffee and cocoa;
or (c) the Government, under its minimum price purchasing program for grains
and beans (which have to be delivered by the producer to stores located
in the main towns). Due to high transportation costs, lack of medium term
financing and inadequate on- or near-farm storage and drying facilities,
monopsonistic power is great, and farmers obliged to sell to intermediaries
at the farm gate receive a much lower price than they potentially could.
7.11      A number of actions considered under or in parallel with the
project should help reduce losses and substantially improve and then stabilize
farmgate prices: (a) access roads would be built or upgraded and maintained
to all-weather standards; (b) warehouses and grain drying facilities would be
built, inland from the main highway, at strategic locations within the project
area; (c) the Government minimum price purchase and loan schemes would be
expanded and simplified, to service a larger number of small farmers; 1/
(d) credit access would be broadened, increasing the small farmer's range of
marketing options and improving his bargaining position; (e) farmers would be
encouraged to build simple on-farm stores; and (f) the agricultural extension
service would provide price and other market information to farmers.
VIII. FINANCIAL ANALYSIS
A. Illustrative Farm Types
8.01      Analysis of probable on-farm development in the subproject area
was based on four illustrative models structured to reflect the impact on
farmer income of the planned development of the major crops sponsored under
the project. Since it is a fundamental project objective to concentrate
agricultural services and credit on the less developed segment of the area
population, whether owner or sharecropper, each model assumes a "without-project"
scenario which develops from the present situation -- as measured in surveys and
studies conducted by INCRA, CEPA-RO, ASTER-RO, EMBRAPA, FAO and the Brazilian
1/   Government authorities are aware of the further need to simplify admin-
istrative procedures in order to give small farmers full access to the
minimum price scheme and are considering measures to that effect.



- 44 -
Institute of Geography and Statistics (IBGE) -- of farmers who have been Iivirng
on their standard 100 ha plots in the settlement areas an average of two years
before project sponsored activities begin to affect them, and who would,
without the project, expand their cropped area at a slower rhythm and with
less productive methods than if the project were to reach them.  Assistance
under the subproject would provide the farmer with the resources and know-how
to plant a minimum area of perennial crops (3 ha of coffee, 5 ha of cocoa or
5 ha of rubber), which should allow him to "get off the ground." The estimatted
financial rate of return for the farmer would range from 22% to over 50%. It
is to be expected, however, that these farmers would eventually expand their
cultivated area beyond this minimum, financing it with their retaineL earnings
or further credit and would thus further enhance the overall economic impact
of the subproject. A description of the four farm types, including the
agro-technical assumptions, operating results, cash flows and monthly labor
use, is provided in Annex 4.

8.04      The proposed farm types envision a minimum level of development,
sufficient to ensure the small farmer's success and stability. As such,
the incremental manpower will be provided mainly by the farm family and the
sharecropper on the coffee farm, with an additional 150 worker-days per year
to be hired on the cocoa farm at full development, and 390 worker-days at
the rubber farm. Details of monthly labor requirements based on FAO field
surveys in the subproject area are given in various tables of Annex 4.
(Volunteer farmer labor contributions to community works envisaged under
the project would be required mainly during the project investment period,
when tree crops would not yet be producing, and on-farm labor needs would be
relatively low. By the time the project reaches full development, community
labor would be needed only for limited maintenance activities.) Global
subproject employment generation is described in para 9.09. Development,
over and above the minimum levels postulated here, will generate a strong
demand for hired (or sharecropper) labor in quantities depending on the
type of crop chosen for expansion. Rubber would generate by far the heaviest
incremental labor requirements per hectare. Although sharecropping arrange-
ments have evolved only for coffee and cocoa so far, they would probably
develop for rubber as plantations grow. Sharecropper terms are very lucrative
at present, but will probably decrease as more labor becomes available through



- 46 -
continuing immigration. These trends would be monitored through the life of
the project, so that measures can be sought to ensure fair returns to share-
croppers, should such measures become necessary.
8.05      The representative farm budgets reflect on-farm investments (includ-
ing land clearing and preparation, establishment of pasture and permanent tree
crops, purchase of livestock, pigs and poultry, minor tools and equipment) and
expected operating and maintenance costs, including fertilizers, soil con-
servation and disease control. Prices for inputs, labor and production were
assumed to be at the average level prevailing over the last three years, in
real terms, modified to take into account year-round access, improved market-
ing conditions and reduced transportation costs resulting from access roads
construction. Transportation cost savings resulting from the pavement of
BR-364 and construction of feeder roads, not taken into account in this
analysis, were taken into account in the evaluation of the overall Northwest
program (see para 2.08).
B. Producer Income
8.06      The present income of the targeted beneficiary families of the
project is estimated to be equivalent to about US$850 in 1981 terms. This is
below the rural relative poverty level for Brazil which is about US$330 per
capita or US$1,580 for the average 4.8 member family of the subproject area
(where, furthermore, the cost-of-living is higher than in most other rural
areas of Brazil). With the project, target farmers, most of whom are strug-
gling today, are expected to increase their family income to the equivalent of
a weighted average of about US$11,900 in 1981 terms. Without the project
(a) development is .xpected to occur at a much slower rate; (b) the average
income generated at "full development" would be considerably lower; and
(c) there would be a large disparity of income between owners and sharecroppers
on one hand and, on the other hand, between farmers located near already exist-
ing services and infrastructure and those further away from BR-364. Many of
the less advantaged farmers would probably gradually leave the area, transfer-
ring their land to their more successful neighbors or outside land speculators,
as has been observed in areas of earlier and less organized settlement.

the value of on-farm consumption of subsistence products are given below:
