- 115                          ANNEX 7
BRAZIL
NORTHWEST REGION DEVELOPMENT PEOGRAM
FIRST PHASE
AGRICULTURAL DEVELOPMENT AND ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION PROJECT
ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION AND RESEARCH
1.        Development of the Northwest region will, unavoidably, increase
pressures on a tropical forest environment which, notwithstanding large areas
of soils of better quality than those found elsewhere in the Amazon basin,
is generally considered to be fragile and has not yet been fully studied.
Concern has been expressed on the possible negative side effects of rapid land
clearing, such as the destruction of possibly unique genetic resources and
ecosystems, the erosion and degradation of fragile soils, the poor utilization
of timber resources and the development of ecologically inappropriate activ-
ities. In an attempt to monitor and minimize such risks and increase the
knowledge of the region, a number of activities are being considered for
implementation within the Agricultural Development and Environmental Protection
Project, with respect to forestry development; establishment of natural parks
and biological reserves; construction of ecological stations; and financing of
various lines of ecological monitoring and research.
A. Forestry Development
2.        Forestry development in the Northwest region would be enhanced by
(a) identifying and reserving large tracts of areas as National Forests for the
subsequent establishment of commercial forestry activities; and (b) strengthen-
ing the forest products marketing control and warehousing infrastructure of
the region.
3.        Responsibility for the forestry development, marketing control,
warehousing and forest protection components would be with the Brazilian
Forestry Development Institute (IBDF), an autonomous organ of the Ministry of
Agriculture, created in 1967 and charged with the formulation of forest policy
and directing the national use and conservation of renewable natural resources
within the rules established by the Forestry Code of 1965 and the Fauna
Protection Law of 1967. IBDF headquarters are in Brasilia. It has five
executive departments (Research; Reforestation; Forest Economics; National
Parks and Reserves; and Industry and Commerce) and a delegation in each state.
Each Park or Reserve has its own director, who, however, usually does not live
within the Park limits. Within the Department of Forest Economics, there is
a Division of Remote Sensing and Monitoring, coordinated from Brasilia, with
laboratories in Belem and Brasilia and a technical assistance group in Manaus.



- llfi -                   ANNEX 7
Page 2
4.        IBDF is a relatively weak organization, which has yet had little
lnfluence on Amazon development and has failed to exert adequate control
over deforestation. Nevertheless, IBDF activities have grown consistently
in recent years and its reforestation (435,000 ha planted 4n 1980 mainly
for charcoal supply) and conservation programs are strong. The conservation
program has 23 National Parks (8.3 million ha) and 11 Biological Reserves
(1.2 million ha).  Ten of these units, totalling 7 million ha, were created
only in the past two years, putting the limited resources of IBDF under severe
stress. In 1980, IBDF had a budget of Cr$ 1.6 billion (US$32 million) and a
staff of some 3,400 (of which some 2,900 are in the state delegations and
500 at headquarters) including some 450 professionals. IBDF has only 15 staff
in Rondonia (of which four are professional level) and 53 in Mato Grosso
(of which three are professional level). This compares with an average of
113 total and 12 professional in other states. The IBDF control point in
Vilhena is paid from territory funds and no control is provided in Rondonia of
existing conservation units and of the rule which provides that 50% of any
property must be left in its natural forest state.
National Forests
5.        There are millions of hectares of virgin forests in the Northwest
region, mostly to be preserved as national parks and biological reserves,
and some of which could be developed into productive national forests. The
creation of such forest estates is necessary to maintain the ecological
integrity of the region and to guarantee an adequate supply of forest products
for domestic and international markets. The establishment of national forests
would be achieved in a number of phases. A working group within IBDF's
Economics Department has identified, in collaboration with INCRA, areas
suitable for commercial forestry operations. The next phase woud be to
delineate those areas and establish small teams of rangers to protect them
from invasion with assistance from aerial patrols. Subsequent phases of
National Forest development would involve the detailed inventorying of the
potential areas, the development of management plans (including the possibility
of forest colonization in which settlers derive most of their income from
sustained selective harvesting or the leasing of larger tracts of state-owned
forest to private operators) and the construction of basic access and admin-
istration infrastructure.
6.        The first phase could be initiated immediately.  The IBDF working
group would use information from satellite images, aerial photographs, fly-
overs and field visits to confirm the potential of a 300,000 ha area of prime
forest land which has already been identified for forestry development purposes
and which would not be of priority use for agricultural settlement. Selection
studies would include forest type, commercial volumes of timber available,
topography and accessibility. The area would be mapped and declared a legal
reserve. It would then be protected by ranger patrols wnile IBDF studies it
in detail to identify those areas within the reserve most appropriate for
timber production. Remaining areas could be considered for later development
or kept as areas of permanent preservation. Areas selected for production
would be the subject of detailed technical, administrative, financial and
economic feasibility studies. The Envirornmental Protection subproject would



- 117 -                         ANNEX 7
Page 3
finance the costs of identification, delimitation, patrolling, inventory
and feasibility studies for the commercial development of the specific area
selected. Corresponding costs are shown in January 1981 terms in US dollars
in Table 1 of this Annex.
Forest Product Control
7.        IBDF has the responsibility of monitoring forest products movements
within Brazil and charges a tax on the products being shipped. The system
has, however, always suffered from shortages of human and financial resources
and poor administration, resulting in under-estimated production statistics
and substantial losses in revenues. There are today two control posts in
Rondonia, one at Vilhena and the other on the road from Porto Velho to Manaus.
The project would construct two new posts in Rondonia (Pimenta Bueno and
Ji-Parana) and improve the Vilhena post. In Mato Grosso, two new posts would
be built (Vila Bela and Aripuana) and two would be improved (Barra do Bugres
and Caceres). Corresponding costs are detailed in Table 2 of this Annex. The
costs of two nurseries to be established in Cuiaba and Porto Velho are shown
in Table 3 of this Annex.
B. Natural Reserves
8.        Elements of justification for the establishment of natural reserves
in the Northwest region are provided in para. 4.05 of the main text of this
report. IBDF's proposed conservation units in the Northwest region are listed
below.
Name                 Type              Area (ha)               Status
Pacaas Novos       National Park            764,800       Created, undemarcated
Guapore            Biological Reserve       500,000       Awaits decree
Jaru               Biological Reserve       268,150       Created, undemarcated
Cara-Cara          Biological Reserve        70,000       Created
The Cara-Cara Biological Reserve, located in the Pantanal area, is intended to
be absorbed within a larger (250,000 ha) Pantanal National Park, for which no
proposal has yet been formulated by IBDF. The project would, therefore,
assist in the establishment of the other three natural reserves.
The Pacaas Novos National Park
9.        While the Park exists officially, everything to secure its integrity
still remains to be done. The Pacaas Novos Park lies in the general area
where uru-eu-wau-wau Indians are known to dwell. Access to a large part of
the area allocated by a Presidential decree to the Park has been interdicted
by a FUNAI decree (portaria), until the on-going process of contacting uru-eu-
wau-wau tribes is completed. Once the tribes contacted, FUNAI may wish to
establish a permanent Amerindian reserve in part of the interdicted area. It
is, however, in the common interest of both institutions that the general area
of the Park be immediately protected. Once an agreement is reached between
IBDF and FUNAI on the location, implementation procedures and future ownership
of the proposed investments. the project would finance the establishment of



- 118 -
ANNEX 7
Page 4
all the infrastructure, housing, roads and airstrips, as well as equipment for
the Park Headquarters and operating costs. The Park is long and narrow, lying
on the watershed of the Guapore and Ji-Parana/Madeira drainage, at between 400
and 500 m elevation. It is the dominant feature of the Rondonia landscape
and, with its high plateau surrounded by cliffs or steep slopes, is physically
rather isolated from the plain below. It is extremely significant in conserva-
tion, since three areas (totalling some 42,000 ha) of fertile soil bearing
open forest of the type being largely taken away by colonization are found
within its boundaries, giving them a critical role in supporting the fauna and
flora endemic to Rondonia. These areas are on the northern or eastern margin
of the National Park, and consequently in urgent need of demarcation and the
establishment of guard posts to avoid penetration from nearby colonization
projects. The cost of establishing the Pacaas Novos park is detailed in
Table 4 of this Annex.
The Guapore Biological Reserve
10.       The Guapore Biological Reserve, originally envisaged in the south-
eastern corner of Rondonia, has since been relocated further west, in lieu of
the Pedras Negras forest reserve. Its size, in the latest context, would be
of the order of 500,000 ha. Creation and demarcation of this reserve await
agreement with the Territorial Government and INCRA on the status of pockets
of agricultural activity along the Guapore River, and with FUNAI, which is now
in the process of delimiting the Rio Branco reserve, immediately to the North.
The reserve is highly varied, including areas of seasonally inundated grassland
in the south, some forest of poor quality on Quaternary deposits and, along
its northern margin, some forest associated with the fertile soils on the
southern flank of the Serra dos Parecis. Information on the reserve is minimal,
since access is extremely difficult other than near the Guapore River, but the
northern forest is expected to perform a useful conservation role for the
flora and fauna endemic to the Guapore, which are clearly distinguished from
those elsewhere in Rondonia, to the north and west. Actual development of the
Guapore Reserve would start on Year 2 of the project to give time to IBDF to
complete an agreement with INCRA and FUNAI.
The Jaru Biological Reserve.
11.       The Jaru Biological Reserve lies between the Rondonia/Mato Grosso
border and the Ji-Parana River. The reserve was redesigned in 1979 in place
of a larger area in which various invasions had occurred. It is at present
under no pressure from the agricultural frontier, but since it has already
once been reduced in area, there is some urgency for demarcation and the
establishment of permanent IBDF headquarters in the reserve. Jaru is varied
because of its strong physiognomic features in the east and has a significant
role in conservation. The forest is, in general, a moist tropical open form-
ation, the only breaks occurring on the crests of hills in the east. The
soils are not particularly suitable to agriculture, but the demarcation prob-
lem may prove difficult. The eventual threat of invasion from agricultural
developments in the Aripuana region of Mato Grosso is probably more serious
than that from the Rondonia settlements. Investments in the Jaru Reserve
would be initiated immediately. The cost of providing the Guapore and Jaru
Reserves with basic infrastructure is detailed in Tables 5 and 6 of this
Annex.



- 119 -                         ANNEX 7
Page 5
C. Ecological Stations
12.       There are at present nine ecological stations in Brazil, two of
which are in the Northwest region, consisting of laboratories, living quarters
and, where required, a landing strip, built by the Special Secretariat for
the Environment of the Ministry of Interior (SEMA) on land which it also owns.
Fourteen universities have current research programs in these stations, dealing
mainly with comparative studies in developed environments. The stations also
serve as bases for a variety of practical ecological work and descriptive
research. Up to 10% of the area of each station is permitted to be modified
for experimental purposes, thus allowing the study of biomass regrowth and
succession. The remainder of the area has to be fully protected in its
present state. SEMA itself does not conduct research, but research programs
have to be agreed with SEMA before a university may use the station. The two
existing and two proposed stations for the Northwest region are listed below:
Name            Area (ha)                     Status
Taiama                 12,000        Established, extension of infrastructure sought
Serra das Araras       24,800        Acquired, no development yet
Ique                  200,000        Established, extension of equipment sought
Cunia             to be negotiated  To be created
(100,000)
13.       Taiama is a heavily used station in the northern Pantanal; the
proposals are for the extension of the facilities to avoid overcrowding. The
station is used by the Federal Universities of Sao Paulo, Minas Gerais and
Mato Grosso, the early work being of a descriptive nature. It is the only
base in the Pantanal providing facilities for field studies.
14.       Serra das Araras lies on the southern fringe of the Barra do Bugres
forest zone, and has a highly varied vegetation because of its transitional
status. Its acquisition is extremely recent. It has been demarcated, and
INCRA is in the process of transferring the property to the Union. The project
would finance all the works attendant on establishing a station. Intimate
mixtures of "cerrado" and forest environments are very diverse, in terms of
both flora and fauna, and this station, lying at the conjunction of two great
vegetation formations, is expected to provide very varied research opportun-
ities, particularly on the limiting factors in these systems.
15.       Ique, in northern Mato Grosso, lies outside the Northwest region,
but is on its eastern margin. By general concensus, it has been included in
the Environmental Protection subproject. This station lies in the Amazon
forest, close to savannah areas, and, as far as is known, is covered in 'dry
forest', a transitional form which is widespread in the Xingu-Araguaia transi-
tion zone, but which is not a feature further west than Vilhena. This station
is under-used, but, again because of the transitional features of the vegeta-
tion, is likely to be a particularly useful site for study of limiting
factors. Financing would cover mainly equipment and the construction of
guard posts.



- 120 -                          ANNEX 7
Page 6
16.       The proposed site for the Cunia station lies on the Madeira River,
north of Porto Velho. The exact area is still the subject of negotiations
between INCRA, Territorial authorities and SEMA, but a positive outcome is
assured. Only the size of the station is still in question. The general
area of the Cunia Station, some 200,000 ha where sandy soils prevail, was
originally intended to be auctioned by INCRA in 2,000 to 3,000 ha plots for
livestock breeding purposes. SEMA now expects to reach an agreement with
INCRA by which half of the original auction area would be set aside for the
establishment of the ecological station. The station gives access to purely
Amazonian landscapes, which include some inundated land as well as forest on
"terra firme." Within the Northwest region, it would make a unique and
specialized contribution to the conservation efforts. The cost components
of establishing or expanding these stations are detailed in Table 7 of this
Annex.
17.       The implementing agency for the Ecological Stations component
of the Environmental Protection subproject will be SEMA, an autonomous
agency of the Ministry of Interior. SEMA has a limited budget of Cr$ 266
million (less than US$3 million) 1/ for 1981 and a staff of about 170,
two thirds of which are employed as consultants. The ecological stations
are run by about four people, none of them scientists or technicians. The
Director usually lives in some nearby town. The project would considerably
strengthen SEMA's capacity to develop and manage ecological stations.
D. Ecological Research
Background
18.       Tropical forests show high variability in soils, water distribution
and climatic patterns. Scientific understanding of the interrelationships
between soil, water, climate and forest interactions in humid forests have
been severely limited by the small number of trained scientists and the
smaller number of research institutions in the tropics in general, and the
Amazon basin in particular. The lack of scientific studies is especially
significant in the POLONOROESTE program area. Research on tropical ecosystems
in Brazil has centered around two locations in Amazonia: Belem and Manaus.
The largely unsettled Northwest did not provide reasonable access for scientific
expeditions until the construction of the unpaved Cuiaba-Porto Velho highway
(BR-364) in the 1960s. Access remained difficult even following completion
of BR-364 and, besides, the institutional emphasis of Amazonian research had
already been focused elsewhere. As a result, there is still much to be
learned about the plant and animal species or ecosystem functions of the
region. The provision of baseline ecological studies as soon as possible for
Rondonia is especially important in order to ensure that any possible negative
impact as program development accelerates is quickly recognized and, as
1/   Another Cr$127 million (US$1.3 million) were received from the Ministry
of Interior budget and various special programs. A further Cr$68 million
are being negotiated with FINEP, the Government agency which finances
development project preparation.



ANNEX 7
- 121 -                          Page 7
feasible, remedied. The results of hydrometeorological and nutrient cycling
studies, for example, would complement on-going soil surveys in determining
which areas are most suitable for future agricultural settlement and would
Research subproject is designed to detect changes in ecosystem behavior, so
that remedial actions can take place at the earliest and least costly stage.
The research would also contribute to a better understanding of the complex
biological and non-biological relationships of the moist tropical forest.
19.       It is generally believed that about half of the water falling
in the Amazon basin as rain originates not in the ocean -- as is true in
most parts of the world -- but from within the basin itself. This implies
that any major change in evapo-transpiration, such as would be produced
by widespread forest clearing, might have major climatic effects. The
climate of the Amazon basin is also believed to have been much drier than at
present during the recent geologic past. During that period, the Amazon
forests receded into isolated patches, each of which developed a distinctive
and very diverse ecology. When the climate reverted to its present wet state,
the forest patches (known as refuges) remerged, but the ecological distribu-
tions retained their patchy pattern, centered on the former refuges. Several
of these refuges were in Rondonia. For this reason, Rondonia is expected to
have many species of plants and animals that live nowhere else. Finally, the
Amazon basin is one of the major stands of uncut timber in the world, and
hence stores much of the world's supply of carbon. Released into the -atmos-
phere as carbon dioxide by burning or by decomposition, this carbon could have
a deleterious effect on the world's climate. On the other hand, a careful
study of the effects of future forest clearing may indicate that this effect
will be less serious than is now feared.
20.       Three main lines of activities are considered to be financed under
the Ecological Research subproject:
(a) hydrometeorology and biogeochemical balances;
(b) biological inventories and minimum ecosystem size studies; and
(c) small scale alternative technology studies.
Hydrometeorology and Biogeochemical Budgets
21.       This component of the Ecological Research subproject would estab-
lish permanent hydrometeorology and biogeochemistry stations in Rondonia for
measurement of the temperature, relative humidity, insolation, evaporation,
precipitation, erosion, streamflow, sedimentation and water chemistry.
There would be at least 19 river stations measuring precipitation and stream-
flow at each station and sedimentation and water quality at selected stations.
There would also be ten standard meteorological stations at off-river locations.
In addition, 18 special stations would be established to collect water samples
for physico-chemical analysis. The stations would conform to standards
established by the National Department of Water and Electrical Energy (DNAEE)
and System of Hydrometeorological Information (SIH). The stations would be
located and operated in cooperation with DNAEE. Physico-chemical analyses
would be performed by CENA and INPA. Groundwater studies would be initiated
by the Federal University of Mato Grosso through a system of monitor wells



- 122 -                          ANNEX 7
Page 8
in each colonization zone, with periodic analyses of water table level
and water quality. The system would be supplemented by periodic monitor-
ing of soil and sediment samples. Finally, as a background to the study
of the biogeochemistry of watersheds, water samples would be taken
throughout the Amazon system to be analyzed for their organic content to
help to understand the flow of organic material from tributaries to the main
channel of the Amazon and thus to provide a global comparative perspective
witnin which to interpret the more detailed results obtained. The five year
base cost in July 1981 prices of this component would be of the order of
US$2,000,000.
Minimum Ecosystem Size and Dynamics of Diversity
22.       This type of research could complement similar existing INPA studies
near Manaus. Support of the projects on these topics would carry a cost of
about US$200,000 per year during five years for a total cost of US$1,000,000.
Biological Inventory and Collection
23.       This component would finance rapid flora and fauna surveys in the
areas of most fertile soils which will be quickly exploited by the agricultural
settlements. Zoological expeditions would collect mammals, birds, reptiles,
fish and insects and study potential disease vectors and reservoirs. Collections
would be made during wet and dry seasons in four major sampling areas, including
the Chapada dos Guimaraes, a famous 19th Century collection site. Each expedition
team would consist of two professional scientists and six to eight assistant-
trainees and would cost about US$500,000 for a four year collection period.
One botanical expedition of three months would be carried out each year over
a four year period with different months of collection to cover each month of
the year. Equipment and personnel costs for the five year period (allowing
one additional year for herbarium work) would cost about US$400,000. Training
costs for assistant-trainees are included in this program.
Appropriate Technology
24.       This component could fund research on appropriate technology.  For
example, an ethno-biological survey of the project area would be made to deter-
mine uses by the local population of biological materials for fuel, fiber,
medicine or food. A few of the species with the most promising commercial
potential might be selected for additional genetic, biochemical and propagation
studies by such agencies as INPA and the University of Mato Grosso (UFMT).
Species which can be propagated successfully will be improved genetically
through standard breeding programs for reintroduction into the project area
for commercial exploitation by small producers. The cost of this program over
the five year period is roughly estimated at about US$750,000. In addition,
research could be conducted on such topics as the use of solar energy for the
control of humidity and temperature, both major restraints on crop growth
and storage, using local material and technology. The cost of such programs
over the five year period would be about US$500,000. Since appropriate
technology research can be initiated quickly by smaller research institutions,
such as the University of Rondonia and the University of Acre, CNPq needs the



- 123 -                          ANNEX 7
Page 9
flexibility to encourage inexpensive, short-term research projects. As
a part of this component, CNPq will have US$50,000 for each of the first
two years and US$100,000 for the remaining three years in uncommitted
funds to support short-term research projects in appropriate technology,
for a total of US$400,000 over the five year period.
Implementation of the Ecological Research Subproject
25.       A major purpose of the research to be supported by the subproject
is to affect decisions regarding the settlement of the region and the con-
servation effort that must accompany it. This fact imposes urgent deadlines
on some features of the work. For this reason, and allowing for some degree
of flexibility because research of the kind envisaged here cannot be put on a
rigid schedule, the hydrometeorological and biogeochemical monitoring system
would be planned so as to generate baseline data and a degree of fundamental
understanding of the hydrology of the region within two years and usable data
on changes due to settlement within another two years. The quick biological
inventories would be completed within four years, although research and follow-
up collections are expected to continue long after this project. First results
of the minimum ecosystem study near Manaus are now becoming available, and
should begin to affect the planning of ecological reserves within a year.
26.       Scientific and technologic research in Brazil is coordinated by
CNPq, with headquarters in Brasilia. This is a relatively new function for
CNPq, which until 1979, functioned basically as a research funding agency,
primarily for university-based researchers. CNPq has already convened a
general meeting of scientists concerned with Amazonian research to define the
problems and priorities of humid tropics research. Responsibility for each of
the researcb subcomponents to be financed as part of the POLONOROESTE program
would be assigned by CNPq in accordance with its coordinating responsibility
after it evaluates proposals it receives from researchers wishing to partici-
pate, for their scientific merit, their cost-effectiveness, their feasibility
under prevailing constraints of manpower and managerial and institutional
capability, and their likely contribution to the POLONOROESTE program and the
overall development of Rondonia. For those research projects which are to
contribute to the monitoring of the effects of settlement, an important
additional criterion would be whether results are likely to be available in
time to influence decisions regarding agricultural land use within the project
area.
27.       Research in hydrometeorology and biogeochemical budgets is part of
the work program of the Center for Nuclear Research in Agriculture (CENA) and
INPA, which receive ancillary support from the National Institute of Space (INPE),
the National Meteorological Institute, DNAEE, and EMBRAPA. INPE, DNAEE and
SUDAM have considerable experience with hydrometeorology stations in Amazonia.
In addition, INPA maintains a model basin near Manaus and has expressed a willing-
ness to establish a comparable basin in Rondonia. INPA is also already involved
in a major experiment on minimum ecosystem size in cooperation with the World
Wildlife Fund. The ecology department of the University of Brasilia is
interested in pursuing research of ecological diversity indicators, and the



124 -
ANNEX 7
Page 10
subject will be of interest to researchers in other parts of Brazil as well.
Taxonomic survey and collection techniques have been developed by INPA and
Museo Goeldi in Belem. Taxonomic expertise of specific plant and animal
groups is found in other Brazilian universities, but INPA and Museo Goeldi
have focused on Amazonian collections. The botanic survey could usefully be
integrated with Project Flora, conducted jointly by the Museo Goeldi and the
New York Botanical Garden. The zoological survey could be integrated into the
newly announced Project Fauna and help get that program underway.
Subproject Cost
28.       Preliminary indications of the order of magnitude of the cost of
various components of the Ecological Research subproject have been given
in various preceding paragraphs. The overall base cost of the subproject
is estimated to be about US$7.0 million including overall coordination and a
reserve for possible new research proposals to be agreed. This figure may
be further ascertained during negotiations, when CNPq will present to the
Bank costed research proposals.

BRAZIL
NORTHWEST REGION DEVELOPMENT PROGRAM
FIRST PHASE
AGRICULTURAL PRODUCTION AND ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION PROJECT
PROTECTION OF AMERINDIAN COMMUNITIES IN THE NORTHWEST REGION
A. The Present Situation
Amerindians in the Northwest Region
1.        Since the colonial period, Brazil's Amerindian population has
fallen from an estimated two to five million to a present-day population of
less than 200,000. This dramatic decline (which is similar to that of other
lowland South American Indians) has been the result of a combination of
factors, including early slave raiding expeditions, intermarriage and, more
recently, with the gradual occupation and development of the Amazon region,
diseases transmitted by new settlers, which have decimated some tribes, and
a reduction of the area available for hunting, fishing and agricultural
activities.
2.        Knowledge of the Northwest Amerindians is still scarce, and infor-
mation from different sources is sometimes contradictory. It is estimated
that some 4,600 Amerindians live in offically designated areas. The total
indigenous population of the area is, however, probably close to 8,000,
including yet uncontacted tribes and groups or individuals living outside the
designated areas. There are more than 20 different tribal groups, some of
which may be interrelated. There are 58 known villages distributed over the
entire program area (26 in Rondonia and 32 in Mato Grosso). Tribal groups
vary considerably with regard to culture, language, economic activities, size
and contact history. The smallest known group has only 10 members, the
largest 1,750. Some groups have been in continuous contact with the Brazilian
population since the rubber boom at the end of the last century, and others,
mainly in the Northeast of Rondonia, have been contacted only since the
construction of the Cuiaba-Porto Velho highway in the 1960s. Still other
groups do not maintain regular contacts. An area of some 880,000 ha has been
interdicted 1/ in central Rondonia where the uru-eu-wau-wau tribe is thought
to be located.  A pacification mission made first contact with this tribe in
March 1981. Another area of some 200,000 ha has been interdicted for the yet
isolated Tubaroes-Massaca Indians in the southeast of Rondonia.
1/   Interdiction is an administrative measure prohibiting the entrance of
anyone, except FUNAI staff, into an area believed to be inhabited by
Amerindians. Then, the area can be delimited, i.e. mapped and legally
recognized as an Amerindian reserve. Demarcation follows delimitation.



- 134 -
ANNEX 8
Page 2
3.        Overall some 5.2 million hectares have been designated official
Amerindian areas (i.e., demarcated, delimited or simply interdicted) in the
Northwest region (2.4 million ha in Rondonia and 2.8 million ha in Mato
Grosso). However, not all lands in which Amerindian communities are present
have been declared Amerindian lands, and of those that have been so designated, at
best a third (1.7 million hectares) would appear to have actually been demarca-
ted, a step essential for their protection. The presence of squatters and
other intruders (miners, hunters, rubber-tappers) within Amerindian reserves
has been a recurrent problem. In some cases, designated Amerindian lands are
inadequate to sustain their assigned population and/or are not the traditional
lands used by the groups in question. In some past instances, the National
Indian Foundation (FUNAI) has improperly turned Amerindian areas over to
ranchers, and the interdicted uru-eu-wau-wau area appears on maps to be
juxtaposed in large part with the area reserved for the Pacaas Novos National
Park. Table 1, attached, shows the approximate populations of known Amerindian
groups in the Northwest region. Table 2 provides data on official Amerindian
areas in the region.
4.        Most of the Amerindians of the Mato Grosso portion of the region
are at relatively advanced stages of acculturation with the exception of some
Nambikwara groups and the recently contacted Saluma and Menku Indians. Some
groups engage in partially mechanized agriculture and maintain commercial
relations with the outside society. As elsewhere in the Northwest, the most
important issues in Mato Grosso are related to land rights. The best known
case in point involves the Nambikwara bands of the Guapore Valley. These
bands, which once controlled almost one million hectares, are today in pos-
session of six small reserves totalling less than 200,000 hectares (of which
less than 60,000 hectares have been demarcated) interspersed among a number of
extensive cattle ranches established in the region within the last ten years.
Since a new stretch of the Cuiaba-Porto Velho road is expected to cut through
the Guapore Valley in the near future, the adequate protection of Nambikwara
Indians in the new environmental context has become an international issue. A
special Bank mission visited Brazil and the Guapore Valley in September/October
1980 and reviewed with the Government its findings and specific recommendations
concerning FUNAI activities in Nambikwara areas. The Guapore Valley Nambikwara
consist of some 10 bands, one not yet contacted, totalling some 300 people.
FUNAI has in the past unsuccessfully attempted to move some of these bands
away from their traditional territories to different areas. Sooner or later,
these bands walked back to their areas of origin, but their population mean-
while decreased to less than a third of what it is estimated to have been in
1960. Today, their land, health, economic and social situation is still
tenuous. Even though their number has recently been on the increase as a
result of improved health treatment, 300 0/oo of babies born over the last
five years did not survive their first year. 1/
1/   Infant mortality during the first year averages 82 0/oo in Brazil, between
110 and 120°/oo in Mato Grosso and 138 /oo in the Territory of Rondonia.



- 135 -                        ANNEX 8
Page 3
Amerindian Policies and Legislation
5.        Brazilian policies with respect to Amerindians aim at ensuring
their cultural and physical survival through adequate protection and assistance
while their culture undergoes gradual modifications, with the long term goal
of their eventual emancipation, individually or as communities, from the
present Government tutelage. It is part of the policy that the protection
provided duly recognize the cultural distinctness of the Amerindians and their
need, given their existing economic system, for large areas of land.
6.        The indigenous population of Brazil has its status and rights
defined in a body of legislation comprised of Article 198 of the Constitution
of 1969, the Indian Statute of 1973 (Law No. 6,001), and a series of laws
and decrees enacted between 1967 and 1980 which established and now govern
FUNAI. This legislation was designed to ensure the survival of the Amerindian
population, regulate their contacts and interaction with the larger national
community, and provide adequate time for the acculturation of the Amerindian
society the modern world. The most important parts of the legislation are the
guarantees regarding Amerindian land rights. Prior to the 1969 Constitution,
Amerindians were considered to own the lands they inhabited. Article 198 of
the 1969 Constitution modified this precept. It guarantees to Amerindians the
inalienable right to the use of the lands they inhabit, but grants ownership
to the Union. The Indian Statute calls for protection of native lands from
illegal trespass and gives FUNAI the right to call on the armed forces and on
the federal police for enforcement of this law. It also states that squatters
rights do not apply to Amerindian lands. According to the Indian Statute,
intervention within native lands is permitted through presidential decrees,
but only in special situations, such as fighting between tribal groups,
serious outbreaks of epidemics, national security requirements (including
public works or subsoil deposits of outstanding national interest) or to
repress encroachments upon Amerindian lands. If, for any of the above
reasons, a tribal group must be removed to another area, the native community
is to be assigned an area equivalent to the former one. However, these
qualifications can reduce (and in some cases have reduced) the force of the
legal guarantees provided in the Amerindian legislation.
The National Indian Foundation (FUNAI)
7.        FUNAI was established in 1967, to replace the Indian Protection
Service (SPI), which had been discredited by the existence of widespread
irregularities in the activities of the organization.  FUNAI is administra-
tively linked to the Ministry of the Interior and maintains administrative
headquarters in Brasilia, responsible for coordinating and overseeing the work
of 13 regional delegations, including those of Cuiaba and Porto Velho in the
Northwest. Direct contacts with the indigenous population takes place mainly
at local Indian Posts. The area, population, and number of villages attended
by these posts varies considerably, sometimes encompassing a large area with
a dispersed population. In principle, each post is to be equipped with
a medical dispensary and staffed by a Post Chief, a health attendant, a
teaching aid, a radio operator, and workmen as needed.



- 136 -                          ANNEX 8
Page 4
8.        At present, there are 13 Indian Posts in Mato Grosso and 13 in
Rondonia. However, in 1980, only 12 of the existing posts were staffed,
and a number of Amerindian villages had no post at all. Few of the posts
were equipped or staffed according to FUNAI standards. There was furthermore
a noticeable difference between the quality of FUNAI operations in Rondonia
and Mato Grosso. The 5th Delegation (Mato Grosso) has been poorly managed in
recent years, and its chief has been replaced; FUNAI is now giving special
attention to upgrading the performance of this Delegation. A total of three
mobile health units operate in the Northwest region (one also attending areas
outside the region), each staffed by a doctor, a dentist, a nurse and a
laboratory technician. These teams carry out routine preventive care missions
and emergency missions (e.g., for the control of epidemics). Additional sup-
port for the health posts in the Guapore Valley would be provided from Vilhena.
9.        FUNAI depends on a number of revenue sources for financing its
activities. The most important source is the annual allocation from the
national budget. This source is followed in importance by allocations from
the Social Assistance Fund and PIN/PROTERRA. Funds generated by FUNAI itself
are relatively insignificant. In 1979, FUNAI was budgeted at slightly less
than US$17.5 million equivalent, of which over half was used for administra-
tion. A serious problem in the history of FUNAI has been the chronic shortage
of funds which has forced the postponement or cancellation of important
programs, including the demarcation of Amerindian lands and the training
provided to Indian Post managers.
10.       Various missionary groups, such as the Brazilian Christian Mission,
universities or institutions such as the Summer Institute of Linguistics,
have provided more or less regular health and education services to Amer-
indians in Brazil, in varying degrees of cooperation with FUNAI. There are
also a number of pro-Amerindian pressure groups (Comissoes pro-Indio) organi-
zed in various locations of Brazil, usually around a core of university
anthropologists.
B. Issues Arising from the Implementation of the Northwest Program
11.       The rapid regional development that will ensue from the POLONOROESTE
Program is bound to place increasing pressures on the Amerindian communities
in the Program area. Without adequate protection, such communities are
likely to be increasingly in competition with settlers for land traditionally
used by the Amerindians, and subject to diseases resulting from increased
contacts with non-indigenous people and from the ecological changes associated
with settlement and deforestation. This question has, from the onset, been
identified among the critical issues to be addressed and satisfactorily
resolved prior to the further implementation of the proposed Northwest
Program.



- 137 -
ANNEX 8
Page 5
12.       As a result, there have been extensive reviews by the Bank and
the Brazilian Government authorities of measures which would be required to
minimize the possible negative effects of POLONOROESTE activities on the
Northwest's indigenous population. It has been agreed that FUNAI would give
two areas priority treatment: (a) the protection of Amerindian lands, parti-
cularly those near areas of settlement, through their demarcation and regulari-
zation, and the removal of illegal squatters from such land; and (b) health
care, through stepped up preventive campaigns and increased capacity for
providing effective assistance in instances of infectious diseases. Economic
development measures taking into account the felt needs and conditions of
individual groups would be progressively implemented. It was further agreed
that measures for protecting and assisting the indigenous population must
be synchronized with the implementation of development activities envisaged
under POLONOROESTE and, where appropriate, precede the latter.
C. Proposed FUNAI Activities
13.       In order to mitigate the adverse effects of current and future
development on the Amerindian population in the area, FUNAI is carrying
out, in the context of the POLONOROESTE program, a special project speci-
fically addressed to the needs of these indigenous communities. To this end,
the POLONOROESTE Program provides for additional human and financial resources
to supplement FUNAI's present staff and budget to carry out land demarcation,
health, administration, education, and economic development activities in the
Northwest Region. The description that follows summarizes FUNAI's "Project to
Assist Indigenous Communities in the Area of Influence of the Cuiaba-Porto
Velho Highway, 1980-1985" (hereafter called the "Special Project") as amended
following review and comment by various Bank missions.
Land Demarcation and Regularization
14.       Recent migration into frontier areas of the Northwest and the
consequent patterns of land settlement make the effective protection of
Amerindian lands crucial to the preservation of the welfare of Amerindian
communities. Such protection is essential to ensure the continued subsistence
of these communities while they adapt to the modern world, as well as to
shield them from the initial shocks of contact. The first step to effectively
protect Amerindian lands is to demarcate the borders of reservations.   It is
felt that this would discourage a good deal of the illegal trespassing taking
place in the region, much of which occurs out of ignorance. Demarcation
involves the clearing and marking of a six-meter swath of land around the
reservation. The FUNAI Special Project calls for an estimated 3,941 km of
perimetral demarcation by 1982, which would affect approximately 2.5 million
hectares of Amerindian lands. These areas, as well as all areas previously
demarcated, are to be regularized through inscription in the "Servico do
Patrimonio da Uniao," also by 1982. However, funding delays slowed initiation



138 -                         ANNEX 8
Page 6
of the 1,321 km of demarcation that were programmed for 1980, and the meeting
of overall targets of the Special Project will now require exceptional efforts
if they are to be achieved on time. The total estimated cost in May 1980
prices for the demarcation and regularization component amounts to Cr$165.6
million (US$3.2 million).  This estimate could be revised upwards if new
demarcation requirements were to be identified. Corresponding costs would be
met by the FUNAI budget.
15.       Effective monitoring is also necessary to protect designated tribal
lands from encroachment. To monitor Amerindian lands, FUNAI requires adequate
means (manpower, vehicles, airplanes) to survey these areas regularly.
To evict trespassers, FUNAI must depend on the state and federal police forces
to effect their removal, and on INCRA, to resettle squatters elsewhere. In
the past, both means and support have been inadequate. Under the Special
Project, once the remaining reserve areas are delimited, a preventive early
detection strategy is to be enforced via (a) periodic surveillance on the part
of FUNAI; (b) control on the part of the Amerindians themselves, who are to
report all irregularities to FUNAI; (c) an improved FUNAI internal reporting
mechanism from the Indian Posts to the Delegations, and from the Delegations
to Brasilia; and (d) forceful and swift response on the part of FUNAI with the
backing of the state government, the police, and/or the military.
Health
16.       The health component is intended to upgrade FUNAI's existing health
services with the objective of extending full coverage to all indigenous
groups in the program area. At present, coverage is spread thinly because
of a lack of funds, personnel, equipment and physical infrastructure. The
component emphasizes the strengthening of health services; the strengthening
of health infrastructure is to be carried out only once service oriented
goals have been met. FUNAI has established the following areas of priority:
(a) immunization of all Amerindians in the Program area, as well as non-Indian
groups in the vicinity of Amerindian areas; (b) strengthening FUNAI's capacity
to quickly and effectively respond to epidemics; (c) control of tuberculosis
and malaria; (d) maintenance of adequate supplies and storage of medicine in
infirmaries; and (e) training of Post Chiefs and Indian Health Monitors to
dispense medicine and provide first aid. Specific measures. to be imple-
mented under the health component to address these areas of priority include
(a) upgrading the existing mobile units; (b) the addition of a new agreement
with a hospital in Vilhena; (c) upgrading the FUNAI clinic in Cuiaba; and (d)
training seminars for Post Chiefs and Indian Health Monitors. Once these
priority measures are achieved, the component provides for measures to
strengthen FUNAI's health infrastructure. The estimated cost of the health
component in May 1980 prices is Cr$ 312.3 million (US$ 6.2 million).
Administration
17.       The viability of the Special Project as a whole depends on a sub-
stantial strengthening and improvement of FUNAI's regional and local admi-
nistration. A personal assistant to the President of FUNAI has been appointed



ANNEX 8
-J139 -                       Page 7
Special Coordinator of FUNAI activities within the Northwest program at
Brasilia headquarters to help expedite related activities and improve co-
ordination between FUNAI and other key institutions involved in the program's
implementation and administration. At the field level, the special FUNAI
project will (a) establish Indian Posts for groups that have so far not been
attended on a continuous basis; (b) increase the efficiency of existing
services through improvements in staffing, communications, transport,
maintenance, and equipment of the Indian Posts and Delegations; (c) upgrade
basic infrastructure at the Indian Posts in order to attract and maintain
field staff; and (d) step-up assistance to Amerindians in the project area
through the establishment of a new support base in Vilhena in 1981. The cost
of the administration component in May 1980 prices is estimated to be Cr$298.8
million (US$ 5.97 million). A decision taken by the Minister of the Interior
to advance FUNAI Cr$50 million should facilitate FUNAI activities under its
special project. Finally, special attention and supervision are already being
given by FUNAI to upgrade the performance of their Cuiaba delegation.
Education
18.       Bilingual education is part of the assistance to which Amerindians
are entitled by law. FUNAI's capacity to fulfill this function has been
constrained by the requirement that a given tribe's language be studied prior
to introduction of schooling, and by the termination of the contract with the
Summer Institute of Linguistics under which the Institute was preparing
educational materials for various indigenous groups. Improvements are likely
to require a build-up of capacity over several years. The education component
is to provide start-up salaries for newly recruited teachers, as well as for
maintenance, equipment, and school construction and upgrading costs. It also
foresees the distribution of school lunches in collaboration with the National
School Lunch Campaign. In March 1981, FUNAI held a course for "bilingual
monitors," i.e., native speakers who will learn how to teach their own com-
munities. FUNAT has agreed that (a) teacher training, rather than school
construction, must be given priority; (b) teachers could move among different
Amerindian groups, given that the latter would be unlikely to attend school
on a year-round basis; (c) simple school facilities would be adequate; and
(d) teacher training, which is not part of the Special Project, would be
adequately covered by existing FUNAI programs. The cost of the education
component in May 1980 prices is estimated to be Cr$37.5 million (US$ 750,000).
Economic Development
19.       Economic development projects for Amerindian communities would be
possible, with the objective of gradually replacing traditional subsistence
activities, should the communities so wish. The objective in expanding the
subsistence base would be, first, to enable Amerindians to satisfy their
increasing demand for commercial goods and, second, to prevent regional
development from having a negative impact on tribal subsistence either because
of reductions in game and fish reserves or because of related changes in
trading patterns with surrounding populations. Economic development projects
would vary with the respective degree of acculturation and the specific
resource base of each group. Activities could include measures to increase



- 140 -
ANNEX 8
Page 8
agricultural production and productivity, forest management, the introduction
of livestock, the expansion of extractive activities, the fostering of non-farm
activities, crop processing and marketing. Marketing would be facilitated by
the organization of 18 pre-cooperatives with trading posts (Cantina Reembolsavel).
These Cantinas would provide credit facilities and basic goods for purchase or
trade. The objective of these arrangements is to reduce the need for outside
contacts to a minimum by providing the Amerindians with options for procurement
of goods and marketing services within their communities. A further measure
will be to contract a natural resources manager or agricultural expert for
each Delegation (to train and supervise Indian Post Chiefs) and 20 agricultural
technicians. The cost of the economic development component in May 1980
prices is estimated at Cr$257.2 million (US$ 5.1 million).
D. Future Monitoring Requirements
20.       Implementation of FUNAI's project is fully under way.  The first
tranche of POLONOROESTE funds has been released and the agency was exempted
from the governmental hiring freeze in August 1981. The Government has
offered to provide the Bank with semi-annual reports on the progress of FUNAI
activities in the POLONOROESTE area, including any related information the
Bank may require. Bank staff would be authorized to visit areas under FUNAI
jurisdiction accompanied by FUNAI staff. The efficiency of protection measures
and the impact of the Special Project and of regular FUNAI activities in the
context of regional development will be evaluated directly by FUNAI, and by
SUDECO in the context of the overall POLONOROESTE program. For this purpose,
FUNAI is to develop a strong monitoring and evaluation capacity, by reviewing
its monitoring and reporting arrangements, by strengthening its evaluation staff
and by contracting Brazilian institutions or experts to assist it in this task.
By March 31, 1982, the Federal Government will furnish the Bank with information
on the financing arrangements for such strengthening activities and on the
qualifications, experience and terms of reference of the Brazilian institutions
or experts who shall assist FUNAI in the carrying out of such strengthening
activities. FUNAI would enter into contracts with such Brazilian institutions
or experts by May 31, 1982.
